The Second Argument: Separation as Destruction and Generation
Presents the second argument (separation as destruction/generation) for the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument of the mutakallimun: that if a divisible thing were one and continuous, then any separation would destroy it and generate something else—an impossibility.

Core Idea
If a thing were truly continuous and one, separating it would mean destroying it, which is absurd.

The second argument considers a different scenario: what if the divisible thing were one and continuous (muttaṣil) in its essence? The mutakallimun show that any separation would then have to be an act of destruction and new creation, which is impossible. This lesson explains the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Assume the divisible is one continuous essence
Consider a thing that is one in itself and continuous in its very nature, not yet actually divided.
Continuity means no gaps or actual separations within.
2
Consider what separation would entail
If we separate such a thing, the separation (tafrīq) would destroy that original essence and bring into existence two separate essences that were not there before.
This is because the original continuous essence had no internal divisions; any division would break its identity.
3
Recognize the falsehood of the consequent
But separation is not an act of destruction and creation; it merely divides something that already exists. Therefore, the consequent is false.
The absurdity shows the assumption is false.
4
Conclude the conclusion
Therefore, the divisible thing cannot be one and continuous; it must already have actual parts, otherwise separation would be impossible.
This reinforces the claim that every divisible has actual parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع: حجة المتكلمين على التقسيم. في حجة جمهور (المتكلمين) على مذهبهم ( وهي نوعان : النوع الأول أن نبين أولاً أن كل منقسم أي قابل للانقسام ( له أجزاء بالفعل ) ثم نبين أنها ) أي تلك الانقسامات والأجزاء الحاصلة بالفعل ( متناهية ) فيعلم من الأول أن أجزاء الجسم البسيط حاصلة بالفعل غير قابلة للانقسام ومن الثاني تناهيها ( أما الأول : ( وهو أن كل ما يقبل القسمة فهو منقسم بالفعل (فلوجوه ) ثلاثة : ( الأول : القابل للقسمة لو كان واحداً) في نفسه غير منقسم بالفعل ( لزم انقسام الوحدة والتالي ،باطل، فالشرطية ) أي استلزام المقدم للتالي، لأنه يلزم على ذلك التقدير ( قيام الوحدة ) الحقيقية (بما يقبل القسمة وانقسام المحل يوجب انقسام الحال فيه ضرورة أن الحال في أحد الجزأين غير الحال في الجزء الآخر، والاستثنائية ) أي بطلان التالي ( بينة إذ لا معنى للوحدة إلا كونها لا تنقسم . الوجه ( الثاني : لو كان القابل للانقسام واحدا في نفسه متصلا في حد ذاته ( كان التفريق ( الوارد على ذلك القابل ( إعداما له ) وإيجادا متصلة لغيره ) والتالي باطل. أما الملازمة فلأن التفريق حينئذ إعدام لهوية ) هي حد ذاتها ( وإحداث لهويتين منفصلتين لم تكونا موجودتين في تلك الهوية الاتصالية وإلا كانت منقسمة بالفعل والمفروض خلافه. وقد وجب كون التفريق على ذلك التقدير إعداماً وإحداثاً.
The fourth aim: The proof of the mutakallimun on division. On the proof of the majority of the mutakallimun for their doctrine, and it is of two types: The first type is that we first show that every divisible, i.e., capable of division, has actual parts, then we show that they are finite. So from the first it is known that the parts of the simple body are actual and not capable of division, and from the second their finitude. As for the first: that everything capable of division is actually divided; it has three aspects. First: if the divisible were one in itself not actually divided, division of unity would follow, and the consequent is false. The conditional [claim] is that real unity subsists in what is capable of division, and division of the place necessitates division of what inheres in it because what inheres in one part is not what inheres in another part. The exceptional [claim]—that the consequent is false—is evident since there is no meaning to unity except its being indivisible. Second: If the divisible were one in itself continuous in its essence, the separation that occurs to that divisible would be a destruction of it and a bringing into existence of a continuous thing other than it; and the consequent is false. As for the implication, because separation then is a destruction of an essence that is its very self and a generation of two separate essences that were not existing in that continuous essence, otherwise it would be actually divided, contrary to the assumption. And it has been necessary that separation in that estimation is destruction and generation.
Source pages p. 8

This passage presents the first part of the mutakallimun's proof for their doctrine of the atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The proof is of two types; here we have the first type, which aims to establish two things: first, that every divisible thing (qābil li-l-inqisām) actually has parts (ajzāʼ bi-l-fiʻl), and second, that those parts are finite (mutanāhiya). ?

?

The Third Argument: Distinctness of Sections and the Reply
Argument for actual parts from distinct sections, with a rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for actual parts based on distinct sections, and the counter-reply that these sections are merely mental constructs.

Core Idea
Mental sections do not prove real parts; they only reflect how we think.

Al-Jurjani presents the third argument: the sections of a divisible thing are distinct (e.g., half vs. third), so the parts themselves must be distinct. But the reply argues that these sections are merely mental attributes considered when we suppose division. This lesson examines this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's words set the stage.
Notice that the argument starts with 'الوجه الثالث' and the reply with 'وأجيب عنه'.
2
Identify the core claim of the argument
The argument says that because the section of the half is different from the section of the third, the parts themselves must be distinct. Why would that be?
The text says: 'ذلك يوجب التمايز في تلك الأجزاء بالفعل'.
3
Understand the reply
The reply says the concepts of sections are mental attributes ('أوصاف اعتبارية') that the intellect considers when supposing division. They do not prove actual distinct parts.
The key phrase is 'لا يوجب تمايز محالها إلا بحسب الفرض'.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument and reply show the subtlety of debates about atomism. Distinguishing mathematical concepts from physical reality is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : إن مقاطع الأجزاء) في الأمر القابل للانقسام إليها ( متمايزة بالفعل فإن مقطع النصف غير مقطع الثلث ،ضرورة، وكذا الربع والخمس) وغيرهما من الأجزاء ( بالغاً ما بلغ ) فإن مقاطعها متمايزة بأسرها. ( وذلك ) أي تمايز مقاطع الأجزاء التي يمكن فرضها ( يوجبـ التمايز) في تلك الأجزاء (بالفعل) إذ لو لم تكن الأجزاء متمايزة في الوجود لم تختلف بتلك الخواص المتمايزة. وأجيب عنه : بأن مفهومات المقاطع أوصاف اعتبارية يعتبرها العقل عند فرض التجزئة وذلك لا يوجب تمايز محالها إلا بحسب الفرض أيضا
The third aspect: The sections of the parts in what is divisible into them are actually distinct, for the section of the half is necessarily different from the section of the third, and likewise the quarter, the fifth, and other parts, no matter how far they go; their sections are entirely distinct. And that (i.e., the distinctness of the sections of the parts that can be supposed) necessitates the distinctness in those parts (actually), for if the parts were not distinct in existence, they would not differ by those distinct characteristics. And it was answered: that the concepts of the sections are mental attributes considered by the intellect when supposing division, and that does not necessitate the distinctness of their loci except suppositionally as well.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) for the actual division of bodies. It argues that the sections (maqaati') of parts within a divisible entity are actually distinct: for example, the section of the half is necessarily different from the section of the third, and so on for all possible divisions. Since these sections have distinct properties, their corresponding parts must also be actually distinct in existence; otherwise, the parts would not exhibit these differing characteristics. The author then gives the answer (from the opposing view): the concepts of sections are merely mental attributes (awsaaf i'tibaariyya) that the intellect considers when hypothesizing division. Their distinctness does not imply actual distinctness of the parts in the external world, except according to the same hypothetical reasoning.

Key Terms
الوجه الثالث
مقاطع الأجزاء
متمايزة بالفعل
أوصاف اعتبارية
فرض التجزئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I can distinguish half from third in a body, then the body must have actual separate parts corresponding to those mathematical divisions.
Correction: The distinction between half and third is a mental construct based on hypothetical division; it does not prove that the body actually has parts that correspond to those sections.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the third argument for the actual distinctness of parts?
A) The senses perceive separate parts
B) The sections of halves and thirds are necessarily different
C) The body appears broken when divided
D) The intellect cannot imagine division without parts

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because the distinctness of sections does not prove actual parts; sections are mental constructs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you mentally divided your life into 'sacred' and 'secular' compartments, treating them as though they are actually separate? What would change if you recognized that division is only a mental construct?
?
This lesson shows that a seemingly logical assumption (distinct sections imply distinct parts) can be false. What is one assumption you hold about yourself or your faith that you've never questioned, simply because it 'makes sense'? Will you test it against revelation?
?
The rebuttal reveals that mental attributes do not necessarily reflect reality. In what area of your worship or character have you mistaken a mental label (e.g., 'I am a patient person') for the actual reality, ignoring the evidence of your actions? How can you align the two?

The Two Views on Unity: Existential or Mental?
Al-Jurjani states the basis of the argument and presents two answers from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two perspectives on unity (existential attribute vs mental consideration) and the two initial answers from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Core Idea
Unity is a mental consideration, not a spreading existential property.

Al-Jurjani begins by noting that a certain argument assumes unity is an existential attribute spreading in its locus. However, he states that the apparent truth is that unity is a mental consideration related to the aggregate of the divisible object. He then examines two responses from Sharh al-Maqasid that attempt to salvage the existential view by denying either its existentiality or its spreading.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the initial statement and the two answers.
The passage contrasts two views.
2
Identify the two answers
The commentary mentions two answers: denial of existentiality (man' al-wujudiyya) and denial of spreading (man' al-saraya). Understand what each denies.
These are presented as possible responses to the problem.
3
Reflect on the problem
Why would existential unity lead to a problem? Think about what happens if unity spreads in a divisible locus.
This prepares you for the verification in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الوجه مبني على أن الوحدة صفة وجودية سارية في محلها لكن الظاهر أنها صفة اعتبارية متعلقة بمجموع الأمر المنقسم من حيث هو مجموع فإذا ورد عليه القسمة زالت الوحدة
قوله : (صفة وجودية سارية إلخ) في شرح المقاصد وأجيب بالوحدة من الاعتبارات العقلية، ولو سلم فليست من الأعراض التي تنقسم بانقسام المحل فعلى هذا ما في الشرح في الحقيقة جوابان : منع الوجودية ومنع السراية لكن التحقيق بأن كونها وجودية يستلزم كونها سارية فهي صفة معللة وذلك لأنها إذا كانت موجودة في الخارج كان قيامها في الخارج بالمحل الموجود في الخارج فهو منقسم فيلزم انقسامها. أما إذا كانت اعتبارية كان قيامها في الذهن بمجموع المحل من حيث أنه مجموع. أما إذا لم يعتبره العقل زالت عنه الوحدة ولم يلزم انقسامها وبهذا اندفع ما في الشرح الجديد بأن البديهة لا تفرق بين الأمور الموجودة في الخارج والاعتبارية الموجودة في نفس الأمر فلما جاء في الاعتباري شغل كل المحل لا بطريق السريان جاز في الخارج أيضاً ذلك وإنما قلنا : اندفع لأن الأمور الاعتبارية عارضة للمجموع من حيث المجموع فإذا زالت الحيثية زالت تلك الأمور الاعتبارية بخلاف الأمور الموجودة فإنها عارضة من حيث ذاته المنقسمة لا باعتبار حيثية الاجتماع.
And this aspect is built upon the idea that unity is an existential attribute spreading in its locus, but the apparent is that it is a mental attribute related to the totality of the divisible thing insofar as it is a totality; so when division comes upon it, unity disappears. His saying: '(existential attribute spreading, etc.)' in Sharh al-Maqasid he answered with 'unity is among mental considerations, and even if conceded, it is not among the accidents that divide by the division of the locus.' So on this, what is in the commentary actually contains two answers: denial of existentiality and denial of spreading. But the verification is that its being existential necessitates its being spreading, so it is a caused attribute; that is because if it exists externally, its subsistence externally is by the locus existing externally, so it is divided, hence its division follows. As for its being mental, its subsistence is in the intellect by the totality of the locus insofar as it is a totality. As for when the intellect does not consider it, unity departs from it and its division does not follow. And by this, what is in Sharh al-Jadid is rebutted, that intuition does not distinguish between matters existing externally and mental matters existing in themselves; so when in the mental case it occupies the entire locus without way of spreading, it is also permissible externally. And we said: it is rebutted because mental matters are accidents of the totality insofar as it is a totality; so when the respect is removed, those mental matters disappear, unlike existing matters, for they are accidents of the essence itself in its divisible nature, not by respect of the totality.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of unity (al-wahda). It begins with a statement that a certain argument (probably the first argument) is built on the premise that unity is an existential attribute (sifah wujudiyyah) that spreads (sariyah) in its locus. But the author says the apparent truth is that unity is a mental attribute (sifah i'tibariyyah) relating to the aggregate of the divisible thing insofar as it is an aggregate; when division occurs, unity vanishes. Then follows a commentary (beginning with 'قوله') from Sharh al-Maqasid, which gives two answers: either deny that unity is existential, or deny that it spreads. The author then provides the verification (tahqiq): if unity were existential, it would necessarily spread, making it divisible like its locus. However, if it is mental, it subsists in the intellect by the totality of the locus considered as a whole; when the intellect does not consider that totality, unity disappears and cannot be divided. This refutes an objection from Sharh al-Jadid that intuition does not distinguish between external and mental matters, so what works for mental attribution should work for external. The author explains that mental matters are accidents of the totality; when the respect (al-haythiyyah) is removed, they vanish, unlike external attributes that belong to the essence itself.

Key Terms
الوحدة
صفة وجودية
صفة اعتبارية
سارية
منع الوجودية
منع السراية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think unity must be an existential property that spreads like other accidents.
Correction: This passage shows that unity is a mental consideration, and even if existential, it does not necessarily spread.

Checkpoint
According to Sharh al-Maqasid, what are the two possible answers to the problem?
A) Denial of existence and denial of unity
B) Denial of existentiality and denial of spreading
C) Denial of mental consideration and denial of division
D) Denial of locus and denial of aggregate

Key Takeaway
The two answers from Sharh al-Maqasid provide initial ways to avoid the conclusion that unity is divisible, but the verification will show why the mental consideration is the correct resolution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm 'Allah is One,' do you think of His oneness as a property that spreads through His essence, or as a mental concept that prohibits division? How does your answer affect the way you understand Tawhid?
?
The passage shows that unity disappears when division occurs. Think of a time when you saw a community or a relationship break apart. Did you realize that its unity was only a mental bond? How does this understanding move you to actively preserve unity before it is lost?
?
If unity is merely a mental consideration, what does that mean for your own sense of personal identity? Are you the same person you were yesterday, or is that unity only a mental construct? How does this challenge you to ground your identity in something eternal, like your connection to Allah?

Verification: Unity is a Mental Consideration
Al-Jurjani provides the verification that unity is mental and rebuts an objection from Sharh al-Jadid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verification (tahqiq) that unity is mental and how it refutes the objection from Sharh al-Jadid.

Core Idea
If unity were existential, it would be divisible; being mental, it depends on the intellect considering the totality.

After presenting the two answers, al-Jurjani provides his own verification. He shows that if unity were existential, it would necessarily spread and thus become divisible like its locus. But if it is mental, it subsists in the intellect by the totality, and when the intellect stops considering that totality, unity disappears without division. This rebuts an objection from Sharh al-Jadid that intuitions do not distinguish between external and mental matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the verification argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the two cases: existential vs mental.
The argument is a conditional analysis.
2
Understand the necessity of spreading for existential attributes
Al-Jurjani argues that if unity is existential, it must spread in the locus, leading to division. Why? Because the locus is divisible.
This is the core of the verification.
3
See how the mental consideration avoids the problem
When unity is mental, it exists only in the intellect by considering the aggregate as a whole. When that consideration is removed, unity disappears without being divided.
This refutes the objection from Sharh al-Jadid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

How can you train your mind to always consider the whole of His perfection?
?
The objection from Sharh al-Jadid assumed intuition does not distinguish. Are you sometimes satisfied with how the concept of tawhid feels intuitive, without verifying it through reasoning? What would it cost your faith if you never moved beyond intuition?
?
If mental considerations are not real existents, then your own thoughts about God are not God. Does that humble you? How does it change the way you approach learning aqeedah—as a purification of the mind rather than mere accumulation of ideas?

Second Proof: Infinite Parts Cannot Be Confined
Al-Sharif al-Jurjani expounds the second proof against infinite parts in a finite body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument against actually infinite parts in a finite body, based on the impossibility of confining an infinite magnitude between finite boundaries.

Core Idea
An infinite number of parts cannot fit between two finite boundaries.

Al-Sharif al-Jurjani continues the critique of infinite parts by presenting a second proof. The argument is straightforward: a finite body has boundaries; its parts, if actually infinite, would have to be contained within those boundaries, which is impossible. The only objection—interpenetration of parts—is rejected by common sense. This lesson walks through the proof and its key terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the main argument to the commentary on three subpoints.
The passage includes both the proof and the commentary; the commentary clarifies three key expressions.
2
Identify the main logical premises
The proof has two premises: (1) the body is finite and confined by boundaries, so its parts are also confined; (2) it is impossible for an infinite quantity to be confined between two boundaries. The conclusion is that the parts cannot be infinite.
3
Examine the objection and its refutation
The opponent might claim interpenetration (tadakhul) to avoid the conclusion. The refutation states that interpenetration violates the self-evident truth that the whole is greater than its part.
Common sense (badiha) rejects interpenetration of two physical bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني: أنه أي الجسم الذي نحن بصدده متناه بالحجم والمقدار فهو محصور بين الطرفين المحيطين به وكذا أجزاؤه محصورة بينهما، ( وانحصار ما لا يتناهى بين الحاصرين محال فاستحال أن تكون أجزاؤه الموجودة فيه بالفعل غير متناهية إلا أن يلتزم التداخل فيما بين تلك الأجزاء لكنه مما تشهد البديهة ببطلانه. قوله : ( وكذا أجزاء إلخ) إن أريد انحصارها مقداراً فمسلم وإن أراد انحصارها عدداً ففيه النزاع . قوله : (إلا) أن يلتزم التداخل لا ينفعه لأنه يلزم تناهي الأجزاء المتناهية في الوضع لأنه يقول : إن جميع الانقسامات الممكنة إلى الأجزاء المقدارية حاصلة بالفعل . قوله : ( مما يشهد إلخ ) أي مداخل له حجم أو مقدار فيما له حجم أو مقدار شبهة البديهة ببطلانه لأنه يستلزم بطلان الحكم البديهي الأولى وهو كون الكل المقداري أعظم من جزئه المقداري .
The second aspect: that the body under discussion is finite in bulk and magnitude, so it is confined between the two extremities surrounding it, and likewise its parts are confined between them. And the confinement of what is infinite between two confiners is impossible, so it becomes impossible for its actually existing parts to be infinite, unless one accepts al-tadakhul (interpenetration) among those parts, but common sense witnesses its falsity. His statement: 'And likewise its parts etc.': If what is meant is their confinement in magnitude, it is conceded; but if what is meant is their confinement in number, then therein lies the dispute. His statement: 'Unless he accepts al-tadakhul' does not benefit him, because it entails that the finite parts are finite in position, since he says that all possible divisions into quantitative parts are actual. His statement: 'what common sense witnesses etc.': i.e., the interpenetration of something having bulk or magnitude into something having bulk or magnitude is something whose falsity is witnessed by common sense, because it entails the falsity of the primary self-evident judgment that the quantitative whole is greater than its quantitative part.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the existence of actually infinite parts in a finite body. The argument begins by noting that the body under discussion is finite in size and therefore confined between its boundaries. Its parts, being within the body, are likewise confined between the same boundaries. However, it is impossible for an infinite quantity to be confined between two boundaries. Therefore, the parts that actually exist in the body cannot be infinite. The only escape is to claim that the parts interpenetrate (al-tadakhul), but common sense rejects this because interpenetration of two physical bodies is impossible. The commentary then clarifies three points. First, if 'confinement' of parts refers to magnitude, it is accepted; but if it refers to number, that is exactly the point of dispute. Second, appealing to al-tadakhul does not help the opponent, because it would still entail that the infinite parts are actually positioned in a finite space, which is contradictory. Third, the absurdity of al-tadakhul is evident: it violates the self-evident truth that the whole is greater than its part.

Key Terms

التداخل
البديهة
الجزء
المتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'confinement' of parts refers to countable number rather than spatial extent.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that if confinement means magnitude, it is conceded; the dispute is about numerical confinement.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the second proof claims that actually infinite parts in a finite body are impossible?
A) Because infinite parts would interpenetrate
B) Because infinite parts cannot be confined between two boundaries
C) Because the body would become infinite in size
D) Because common sense rejects infinite division

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that infinite parts cannot exist in a finite body because they would be confined between finite boundaries, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that common sense rejects even the possibility of infinite parts in a finite body. Is there an area in your life where you have been rationalizing something that your inner common sense—your fitra—tells you is false? What would it take for you to stop overthinking and trust that witness?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that the only escape (interpenetration) leads to an absurdity that violates a self-evident truth. Have you ever accepted a comforting escape from a difficult truth, only to realize later that it contradicted something you knew deeply? What did that cost you?
?
The proof hinges on the finitude of your own body and its parts. How does the realization that you are finite—limited in space, time, and capacity—affect your sense of dependence on the Infinite? Does it humble you, frighten you, or free you from the illusion of self-sufficiency?

The Third Proof: Composition Necessitates Finite Parts
Al-Jurjani states the third proof: composition yields finite parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument that joining parts together must increase volume, therefore a body cannot have infinitely many parts.

Core Idea
If parts were infinite, composition would produce zero volume—so parts must be finite.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the third proof against the notion that bodies are composed of infinitely many parts. He begins with a simple principle: composition (joining parts) necessarily increases volume. If it did not, then adding parts would never increase size, and even an infinite number of parts would yield no volume at all—contradicting the obvious fact that bodies have extended volume. This forces the conclusion that the parts of a body must be finite in number.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Read the first line: 'al-ta'lif must produce an increase in volume.' Understand why this is taken as self-evident—otherwise two parts would have the same volume as one.
2
Follow the consequence for infinite parts
If parts were infinite, compounding them would never yield a finite volume because adding infinite parts would not accumulate—the absurd result of no volume at all.
Contrast with the observed reality that bodies have volume.
3
Extract the conclusion
Since composition can produce volume only from finite additions, the parts must be finite. The passage then uses the concept of units in numbers to reinforce that infinite parts imply finite units.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : أن التأليف ) هو ضم بعض الأجزاء الموجودة في الجسم إلى بعض ( لا بد أن يفيد زيادة حجم وإلا لكان حجم الاثنين كحجم الواحد وكذا الثلاثة والأربعة إلى غير النهاية فلا يحصل من تأليف الأجزاء ) وإن كانت غير متناهية (حجم) أصلاً ) والمفروض خلافه ) لأن الجسم له حجم ممتد في الجهات ولا شك أن هذا الحجم لفرط اختلاط الأجزاء البيض في السود بحيث لا امتياز في الحس لأن الأجزاء مصلقون عنها كثيراً، بل لا نسبة لها الأجزاء بالسواد لكونها غير متناهية . إنما حصل له من تأليف أجزائه بعضها إلى بعض ) وإذا كان التأليف يفيد زيادة حجم فليجعل التأليف من أجزاء متناهية في جميع الجهات فيحصل حجم في الجهات ) كلها ( وهو الجسم ) وتوضيحه أن كل عدد سواء كان متناهياً أو غير متناه فإنه يشتمل على آحاد حقيقية أي غير منقسمة بالفعل لأن حقيقة العدد مركبة الأحاد قطعاً والمنقسم بالفعل عدد لا واحد فلو لم يوجد في العدد إلا ما هو منقسم بالفعل لم يوجد فيه الواحد أصلاً فلا يكون عدداً قطعاً، فإذا فرض أن أجزاء الجسم عدد غير متناه فلا شك أن فيها آحاداً متناهية فإذا أخذت تلك الآحاد وضم بعضها إلى بعض حصل جسم مركب من أجزاء متناهية ( فليس كل جسم مركباً من أجزاء لا تتناهى ) فبطل الكلية التي ادعاها النظام فإن قلت : هذا جسم مصنوع وماذهب إليه إنما هو في الأجسام المخلوقة. قلت: ما ذكرناه تصوير له مع كونه موجوداً في ضمن تلك الأجسام إذ لا بد أن ينضم فيها أجزاء متناهية بعضها إلى بعض ( ثم) إذا شئنا أن نبطل قوله بالكلية ( نقول : وهذا الجسم له حجم متناه وأجزاء متناهية والجسم الذي فيه البحث ما له حجم متناه لتناهي الأبعاد ( وأجزاء غير متناهية ) على زعمه (ولا شك أن بحسب ازدياد الأجزاء يزداد الحجم ) لأن حجم المؤلف من الأجزاء هو حجم الأجزاء المؤلفة المقتضية لازدياد حجمه ( فتكون نسبة الحجم إلى الحجم نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء، لكن نسبة الحجم إلى الحجم نسبة متناه إلى متناه ونسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء نسبة غير متناه إلى غير متناه، فالنسبة بين المتناهيين مغايرة للنسبة بين غير المتناهيين فلم يلزم من النسبة الأولى محال، هذا خلف. قوله : ( وتوضيحه إلخ) المقصود منه دفع ما قيل أن النظام لا يقول بوجود الجزء على الانفراد وإنما يكون في ضمن الجسم وحاصل الدفع أنه لا بد من وجود الواحد في تلك الكثرة التي ركب الجسم فإذا أخذ الآحاد المتناهية واعتبر انصمام بعضها ببعض حصل الجسم المتناهي الأجزاء في ضمن ذلك الجسم المتناهي مع كونه موجوداً في ضمن الأجسام المخلوقة لا صنع له، فهو أيضا جسم مخلوق إلا أنه مخلوق في ضمنها قوله : ( أي غير منقسمة إلخ لا بمعنى لا يمكن انقسامه فإن وجوده غير لازم في العدد إذ اللازم وجود ما يتقوم به العدد وهو الواحد بالفعل. قوله : ( لأن حجم المؤلف إلخ) اندفع بهذا ما قيل : إن ازدياد الحجم بحسب الازدياد مع كون النسبتين مختلفتين بل يجوز أن يكون نسبة الجسمين من النسب التي يوجد في المقادير دون الأعداد فلا يوجد مثلها في الآحاد لأن نسبتها عددية وخلاصة الدفع أنه ليس حجم المؤلف على تقدير التركيب من الأجزاء ليس إلا مجموع أحجام الأجزاء المؤلفة لا تغاير إلا بالاعتبار فلا بد أن تكون النسبة في المقدار أي في العظم والصغر كنسبة أجزائهما وما ذكرتم إنما يتم إذا كان العظم والصغر غير تابع لكثرة الأجزاءات وقلتها، وذلك مبني نفي الأجزاء وإثبات الهيولى والصورة .
The third aspect: that composition (al-ta'lif) is the joining of some of the parts existing in the body to others; it must necessarily produce an increase in volume, otherwise the volume of two would be like the volume of one, and likewise three and four ad infinitum. So from the composition of parts, even if they are infinite, no volume at all would result, contrary to what is assumed, because the body has volume extended in dimensions. And there is no doubt that this volume [? due to excessive mixing of white parts and black so that there is no distinction in sense because the parts are created [?] from it much, nay, there is no ratio of the parts to blackness because they are infinite. It arises for him from the composition of its parts to one another. And if composition produces an increase in volume, then let composition be from finite parts in all directions, so volume results in all directions, and that is the body. And its clarification: that every number, whether finite or infinite, includes real units, i.e., actually indivisible, because the reality of a number is composed of units definitively, and what is actually divisible is a number, not a unit. So if only what is actually divisible existed in a number, the unit would not exist at all, and it would not be a number definitively. So if it is assumed that the parts of a body are an infinite number, there is no doubt that it contains finite units. So if those units are taken and joined to one another, a body results composed of finite parts. Thus not every body is composed of infinite parts; so the universality claimed by al-Nazzam is falsified. For if you say: This body is artificial, and what he held concerns only natural bodies? I say: What we have mentioned is a depiction of it, while it exists within those bodies, for there must be finite parts joined together within them. Then, if we wish to falsify his claim universally, we say: This body has finite volume and finite parts, and the body under discussion has finite volume due to finite dimensions, and (its parts are infinite) according to his claim. There is no doubt that with increase of parts, the volume increases, because the volume of what is composed of parts is the volume of the composed parts, necessitating increase of its volume. So the ratio of volume to volume is the ratio of parts to parts. But the ratio of volume to volume is a ratio of finite to finite, and the ratio of parts to parts is a ratio of infinite to infinite. So the ratio between the two finites is different from the ratio between the two infinites; so from the first ratio no impossibility follows; this is a contradiction. His statement: 'and its clarification etc.' The purpose is to rebut what was said that al-Nazzam does not affirm the existence of a part in isolation, but only within the body. The gist of the rebuttal is that it is necessary for there to exist the one in that multiplicity which composes the body. So if one takes the finite units and considers their mutual joining, the body with finite parts results within that finite body, while it exists within natural bodies not made by him; so it too is a created body, except that it is created within them. His statement: 'i.e., actually indivisible etc.' does not mean that it cannot be divided, for its existence is not necessary in the number; rather what is necessary is the existence of that by which the number subsists, and that is the actual unit. His statement: 'because the volume of what is composed etc.' By this is rebutted what was said that the increase of volume according to increase (of parts) is with the two ratios being different; rather it is possible that the ratio of the two bodies is among the ratios that exist in magnitudes but not in numbers, so that a similar ratio does not exist among units because their ratio is numerical. The summary of the rebuttal is that the volume of what is composed, on the assumption of composition from parts, is nothing but the sum of the volumes of the composed parts, differing only by consideration. So the ratio in magnitude, i.e., in bigness and smallness, must be like the ratio of their parts. And what you mentioned applies only if bigness and smallness are not dependent on the multiplicity and paucity of parts. And that is based on denying atoms and affirming hylomorphism.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against actually infinite parts in a body, along with an elaboration and refutation of al-Nazzam. The proof begins with the principle that composition (joining parts together) must increase volume; otherwise, any number of parts would yield the same volume, which is absurd. Consequently, if parts were infinite, their composition would produce no volume at all, contradicting the observed extended volume of bodies. The author then clarifies that if composition increases volume, one can take finite parts and compose them to obtain the body's volume, so not every body is composed of infinite parts. This refutes al-Nazzam's claim that all bodies are composed of infinite parts. The commentary addresses two objections. First, al-Nazzam might say that parts do not exist separately but only within the whole. The author answers that even within the whole, there must be actual units that are finite in number, and by composing them we get a body with finite parts. Second, regarding the ratio of volumes to parts, the commentary explains that the volume of a composed body is the sum of the volumes of its parts, so the ratio of volumes equals the ratio of the number of parts (if parts are uniform). Thus, if one body has finite volume and finite parts, and another body has finite volume yet supposedly infinite parts, the ratios would be mismatched, leading to contradiction. The commentary also clarifies that 'actually indivisible' does not mean indivisible in principle but refers to the unit required for number. It also rebuts the claim that increasing parts does not necessarily increase volume proportionally because the ratios might be different; the author insists that the volume of the composed is simply the sum of the parts' volumes, so the ratio must be the same.

Key Terms

العدد
الآحاد

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental principle that the third proof relies on?
A) Parts are indivisible
B) Composition increases volume
C) Body is composed of atoms
D) Number includes units

Key Takeaway
Composition must increase volume; therefore, infinite parts are impossible because they would yield zero volume.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that every body, including your own, is composed of finite parts. Do you live as if your body is merely a temporary, limited vessel? What would change if you truly felt that every moment you have is finite and will end?
?
Al-Nazzam rejected finite parts to avoid the implications of a Creator. Is there an area in your life where you resist limits—whether in your desires, your plans, or your knowledge—because accepting them would require you to acknowledge your dependence on Allah?
?
If composition necessarily increases volume, then the deeds you accumulate also build a 'volume' on your scale. Are you content with the composition of your actions today? What finite step will you take right now to increase the volume of your good deeds?

4 min
Refuting Al-Nazzam: Parts Exist Within the Body
Al-Jurjani rebuts al-Nazzam's objection about parts existing only in the composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani answers al-Nazzam's objection that parts do not exist separately but only within the body.

Core Idea
Even within the whole, finite units exist and can be combined to form a body.

Al-Nazzam might argue that parts do not exist in isolation; they are only found within the composite body. Al-Jurjani responds by showing that even within the whole, there must be finite units that are actually indivisible. By taking those units and combining them, you obtain a body with finite parts. This counters the idea that all parts are mere potentials within an infinite whole.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection: 'This body is artificial, and what he held concerns only natural bodies.' Understand that al-Nazzam might restrict his claim to natural (created) bodies.
2
Follow the rebuttal
Al-Jurjani says that our argument still holds: within any body, finite units exist. By joining them, a finite body results—even within natural bodies.
3
Note the conclusion
Thus al-Nazzam's universal claim (all bodies have infinite parts) is false, because we can always find finite parts that compose a body.

متناهية ) على زعمه (ولا شك أن بحسب ازدياد الأجزاء يزداد الحجم ) لأن حجم المؤلف من الأجزاء هو حجم الأجزاء المؤلفة المقتضية لازدياد حجمه ( فتكون نسبة الحجم إلى الحجم نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء، لكن نسبة الحجم إلى الحجم نسبة متناه إلى متناه ونسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء نسبة غير متناه إلى غير متناه، فالنسبة بين المتناهيين مغايرة للنسبة بين غير المتناهيين فلم يلزم من النسبة الأولى محال، هذا خلف. قوله : ( Clarification of the Ratio Argument and Hylomorphism
Al-Jurjani clarifies the ratio argument and links it to the atomist-hylomorphism debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed commentary on the ratio argument and the final reference to hylomorphism as the only way to salvage al-Nazzam's view.

Core Idea
The volume of a composed body is just the sum of its parts' volumes; otherwise, one must reject atomism for hylomorphism.

In this concluding part, al-Jurjani clarifies the ratio argument further. He explains that the volume of a composite is nothing but the sum of the volumes of its parts, differing only in mental consideration. Therefore, the ratio of volumes must equal the ratio of the numbers of parts. If someone claims that increasing parts does not necessarily increase volume proportionally, that position rests on denying atoms and embracing hylomorphism—the view that matter and form constitute bodies without atomic parts. Thus, al-Jurjani pinpoints the ultimate philosophical divide.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification
Read the commentary on 'and its clarification etc.' It explains that the volume of the composite is the sum of the volumes of the parts, so the ratio must match.
2
Identify the rebuttal to a potential objection
Someone might say that the ratio of volumes could be different from the ratio of parts. Al-Jurjani answers that this only works if volume is not dependent on the number of parts.
3
Note the underlying assumption
The rejection of the dependence of volume on part-count implies a denial of atoms and an affirmation of hylomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

متناهية ) على زعمه (ولا شك أن بحسب ازدياد الأجزاء يزداد الحجم ) لأن حجم المؤلف من الأجزاء هو حجم الأجزاء المؤلفة المقتضية لازدياد حجمه ( فتكون نسبة الحجم إلى الحجم نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء، لكن نسبة الحجم إلى الحجم نسبة متناه إلى متناه ونسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء نسبة غير متناه إلى غير متناه، فالنسبة بين المتناهيين مغايرة للنسبة بين غير المتناهيين فلم يلزم من النسبة الأولى محال، هذا خلف. قوله : ( وتوضيحه إلخ) المقصود منه دفع ما قيل أن النظام لا يقول بوجود الجزء على الانفراد وإنما يكون في ضمن الجسم وحاصل الدفع أنه لا بد من وجود الواحد في تلك الكثرة التي ركب الجسم فإذا أخذ الآحاد المتناهية واعتبر انصمام بعضها ببعض حصل الجسم المتناهي الأجزاء في ضمن ذلك الجسم المتناهي مع كونه موجوداً في ضمن الأجسام المخلوقة لا صنع له، فهو أيضا جسم مخلوق إلا أنه مخلوق في ضمنها قوله : ( أي غير منقسمة إلخ لا بمعنى لا يمكن انقسامه فإن وجوده غير لازم في العدد إذ اللازم وجود ما يتقوم به العدد وهو الواحد بالفعل. قوله : ( لأن حجم المؤلف إلخ) اندفع بهذا ما قيل : إن ازدياد الحجم بحسب الازدياد مع كون النسبتين مختلفتين بل يجوز أن يكون نسبة الجسمين من النسب التي يوجد في المقادير دون الأعداد فلا يوجد مثلها في الآحاد لأن نسبتها عددية وخلاصة الدفع أنه ليس حجم المؤلف على تقدير التركيب من الأجزاء ليس إلا مجموع أحجام الأجزاء المؤلفة لا تغاير إلا بالاعتبار فلا بد أن تكون النسبة في المقدار أي في العظم والصغر كنسبة أجزائهما وما ذكرتم إنما يتم إذا كان العظم والصغر غير تابع لكثرة الأجزاءات وقلتها، وذلك مبني نفي الأجزاء وإثبات الهيولى والصورة .
The third aspect: that composition (al-ta'lif) is the joining of some of the parts existing in the body to others; it must necessarily produce an increase in volume, otherwise the volume of two would be like the volume of one, and likewise three and four ad infinitum. So from the composition of parts, even if they are infinite, no volume at all would result, contrary to what is assumed, because the body has volume extended in dimensions. And there is no doubt that this volume [? due to excessive mixing of white parts and black so that there is no distinction in sense because the parts are created [?] from it much, nay, there is no ratio of the parts to blackness because they are infinite. It arises for him from the composition of its parts to one another. And if composition produces an increase in volume, then let composition be from finite parts in all directions, so volume results in all directions, and that is the body. And its clarification: that every number, whether finite or infinite, includes real units, i.e., actually indivisible, because the reality of a number is composed of units definitively, and what is actually divisible is a number, not a unit. So if only what is actually divisible existed in a number, the unit would not exist at all, and it would not be a number definitively. So if it is assumed that the parts of a body are an infinite number, there is no doubt that it contains finite units. So if those units are taken and joined to one another, a body results composed of finite parts. Thus not every body is composed of infinite parts; so the universality claimed by al-Nazzam is falsified. For if you say: This body is artificial, and what he held concerns only natural bodies? I say: What we have mentioned is a depiction of it, while it exists within those bodies, for there must be finite parts joined together within them. Then, if we wish to falsify his claim universally, we say: This body has finite volume and finite parts, and the body under discussion has finite volume due to finite dimensions, and (its parts are infinite) according to his claim. There is no doubt that with increase of parts, the volume increases, because the volume of what is composed of parts is the volume of the composed parts, necessitating increase of its volume. So the ratio of volume to volume is the ratio of parts to parts. But the ratio of volume to volume is a ratio of finite to finite, and the ratio of parts to parts is a ratio of infinite to infinite. So the ratio between the two finites is different from the ratio between the two infinites; so from the first ratio no impossibility follows; this is a contradiction. His statement: 'and its clarification etc.' The purpose is to rebut what was said that al-Nazzam does not affirm the existence of a part in isolation, but only within the body. The gist of the rebuttal is that it is necessary for there to exist the one in that multiplicity which composes the body. So if one takes the finite units and considers their mutual joining, the body with finite parts results within that finite body, while it exists within natural bodies not made by him; so it too is a created body, except that it is created within them. His statement: 'i.e., actually indivisible etc.' does not mean that it cannot be divided, for its existence is not necessary in the number; rather what is necessary is the existence of that by which the number subsists, and that is the actual unit. His statement: 'because the volume of what is composed etc.' By this is rebutted what was said that the increase of volume according to increase (of parts) is with the two ratios being different; rather it is possible that the ratio of the two bodies is among the ratios that exist in magnitudes but not in numbers, so that a similar ratio does not exist among units because their ratio is numerical. The summary of the rebuttal is that the volume of what is composed, on the assumption of composition from parts, is nothing but the sum of the volumes of the composed parts, differing only by consideration. So the ratio in magnitude, i.e., in bigness and smallness, must be like the ratio of their parts. And what you mentioned applies only if bigness and smallness are not dependent on the multiplicity and paucity of parts. And that is based on denying atoms and affirming hylomorphism.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against actually infinite parts in a body, along with an elaboration and refutation of al-Nazzam. The proof begins with the principle that composition (joining parts together) must increase volume; otherwise, any number of parts would yield the same volume, which is absurd. Consequently, if parts were infinite, their composition would produce no volume at all, contradicting the observed extended volume of bodies. The author then clarifies that if composition increases volume, one can take finite parts and compose them to obtain the body's volume, so not every body is composed of infinite parts. This refutes al-Nazzam's claim that all bodies are composed of infinite parts. The commentary addresses two objections. First, al-Nazzam might say that parts do not exist separately but only within the whole. The author answers that even within the whole, there must be actual units that are finite in number, and by composing them we get a body with finite parts. Second, regarding the ratio of volumes to parts, the commentary explains that the volume of a composed body is the sum of the volumes of its parts, so the ratio of volumes equals the ratio of the number of parts (if parts are uniform). Thus, if one body has finite volume and finite parts, and another body has finite volume yet supposedly infinite parts, the ratios would be mismatched, leading to contradiction. The commentary also clarifies that 'actually indivisible' does not mean indivisible in principle but refers to the unit required for number. It also rebuts the claim that increasing parts does not necessarily increase volume proportionally because the ratios might be different; the author insists that the volume of the composed is simply the sum of the parts' volumes, so the ratio must be the same.

Key Terms
الهيولى والصورة
النسبة
الآحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'actually indivisible' means the atom is absolutely indivisible.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that it only means the unit is not divisible in the sense of being a component of number; it could be divisible in principle.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the only way to avoid the conclusion that parts must be finite?
A) Denying that bodies have volume
B) Denying atoms and affirming hylomorphism
C) Claiming that parts are infinitely divisible
D) Asserting that numbers are unreal

Key Takeaway
The ratio argument forces a choice: either accept finite parts or abandon atomism for hylomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are confronted with a logical argument that forces a stark choice between two theological positions (like atoms vs. hylomorphism), do you feel compelled to choose based on reason alone, or do you seek guidance from revelation? Which has more actual weight in your heart—and what does that reveal about your reliance on Allah's guidance?
?
Have you ever faced a theological dilemma that seemed to have no middle ground? How did you respond—did you embrace the discomfort and pursue the truth, or did you try to escape it? Reflect on the lesson's insistence that one must pick between atoms and hylomorphism: do you have the courage to accept hard intellectual conclusions for the sake of truth, even when they challenge comfortable assumptions?
?
The ratio argument depends on the idea that complex realities (like a body's volume) are simply the sum of their parts. In your own life, do you tend to reduce complex matters to their components, or do you recognize that Allah's creation may involve deeper principles beyond mere aggregation? How does this affect your understanding of tawheed and the oneness of Allah's creative act?

Second Type of Argument: Composition from Indivisible Parts (First Way: The Point)
Presents the first way: the point as necessary for contact

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument for the existence of indivisible points based on the phenomenon of physical contact.

Core Idea
Contact between bodies forces us to accept the reality of indivisible points.

This passage presents the first of seven ways to prove that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (atoms). The argument starts with the observable fact of contact between bodies and traces the chain of contact down to something that cannot be divided further: the point. This is a key example of how kalam reasoning uses sensible phenomena to demonstrate metaphysical principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow. Notice the dense style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage argues that the point exists and is indivisible. Locate where this claim is stated and how it is justified.
Look for the phrase 'النقطة موجودة'.
3
Trace the argument's chain
Follow the reasoning: bodies contact surfaces, surfaces contact lines, lines contact points. Each step involves contact between two existing things. If any step were not existing, contact would be with nothing, which is impossible.
The argument uses a regress to an indivisible endpoint.
4
Understand the alternative
If the chain did not end at a point, there would be infinite regress or interpenetration. The commentary explains why these are invalid.
The author references earlier discussions about infinity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The point is indivisible because it has no depth – it is the endpoint of division. Reflect on why this supports atomism.
The argument is foundational for the kalam cosmological model.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( النوع الثاني : ( من حجة جمهور المتكلمين على ما ذهبوا إليه ( أن نبين تركب الجسم منها ( أي من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ( ابتداء ) أي من غير استعانة بأن كل قابل للانقسام فهو منقسم بالفعل كما في النوع الأول، وأما كون تلك الأجزاء متناهية فهو ظاهر أو معلوم مما مر آنفا ( وهو وجوه ( سبعة : ( الأول : النقطة ) وهـ تنقسم ( موجودة إذ بها تماس الخطوط والخطوط بها تماس السطوح والسطوح بها تماس الأجسام وتماس الموجودين بالمعدوم ضروري البطلان ) يعني لا شبهة في أن الأجسام موجودة وأنها تماس بأمور موجودة منقسمة في الطول والعرض دون العمق وإلا لزم التداخل بين المنقسمين في العمق أو كون التماس بجزأين منهما لا بهما فينقل الكلام إلى ذينك الجزأين وعدم انقسامهما ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى ما لا ينقسم في العمق وذلك هو السطح فثبت وجوده. ثم إن السطحين الموجودين يتماسان على أمر منقسم في الطول دون العرض، وإلا لزم أحد قوله : ( وتماس الموجودين بالمعدوم إلخ لأن التماس على ما في الشفاء كون الشيئين بحيث يكون طرفاهما معاً في الوضع أي في قبول الإشارة الحسية ولا شك أن المعدوم لا يقبل الإشارة الحسية. قوله : ( لكنه باطل كما عرفت أي من قوله : لكنه مما يشهد البديهة ببطلانه . قوله : (بل بالقوة التي يستحيل خروجها بكليتها إلى الفعل كما مر أي في المقصد الثالث من قوله : إما متناهياً أي واصلاً إلى حد يقف عنده ولا يمكن مجاوزته إياه وإما غير متناه لا بمعنى أن تلك الانقسامات إلخ . قوله : ( وأما كون تلك الأقسام متناهية فهو ظاهر يعني أنه لا بد منه في هذا النوع إلا أنه تركه لظهوره أو لكونه معلوماً .
(The Second Type: from the argument of the majority of the theologians on what they have held: that we demonstrate the composition of the body from them, i.e., from parts that are indivisible, ab initio, i.e., without recourse to the fact that everything divisible is actually divided as in the first type. As for those parts being finite, it is apparent or known from what has just passed. And it has seven ways. (The First: the point.) And it is indivisible? (It exists because by it lines are in contact, and by lines surfaces are in contact, and by surfaces bodies are in contact, and the contact of two existing things with a non-existent thing is necessarily invalid.) Meaning: there is no doubt that bodies exist and that they are in contact with existing things that are divisible in length and width but not depth; otherwise, interpenetration would follow between the two [things] divided in depth, or the contact would be by two parts of them not by them, so the discussion would pass to those two parts and their non-division, and it would not regress infinitely but would end at what is not divided in depth, and that is the surface, so its existence is established. Then the two existing surfaces are in contact with each other upon something divisible in length but not width; otherwise, one [of the following would occur]. His saying: (And the contact of two existing things with a non-existent, etc.) Because contact, according to what is in the Shifa', is the state of two things such that their extremities are together in position, i.e., in receiving sensory indication; and there is no doubt that the non-existent does not receive sensory indication. His saying: (But it is invalid as you have learned.) i.e., from his saying: 'But it is among things that intuition testifies to its invalidity.' His saying: (Rather, with the potential whose complete actualization is impossible, as has passed.) i.e., in the Third Purpose from his saying: 'Either finite, i.e., reaching a limit at which it stops and cannot surpass it, or infinite, not in the sense that those divisions...' etc. His saying: (And as for those parts being finite, it is apparent.) Meaning that it is necessary in this type, but he left it due to its obviousness or because it is known.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of argument used by the majority of theologians to prove that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (atoms). Unlike the first type, which argued from actual division, this type demonstrates composition directly ('ab initio'). The author lists seven ways, and this lesson covers the first way: the existence of the point. The argument proceeds from the observation of contact (tamas) between bodies. Bodies are in contact; this contact must occur through something existing and divisible in two dimensions (a surface). If contact were through something divisible in depth, interpenetration would occur. Thus, contact requires surfaces, which are themselves in contact through lines, and lines through points. The point is indivisible in all dimensions. The commentary explains that contact of two existing things with a non-existent thing is impossible because the non-existent cannot be sensed. The commentary also clarifies that the potential infinite divisibility mentioned earlier does not affect this argument. The finiteness of parts in this type is obvious and thus not labored.

Key Terms
النقطة (al-nuqta)
التماس (al-tamas)
السطح (al-sath)
الخط (al-khatt)
التركيب (al-tarkib)
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ (al-ajza' allati la tatajazza')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point is merely a geometric abstraction with no real existence.
Correction: The argument shows that physical contact forces us to accept points as real existing entities; otherwise, contact would be impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument for the point conclude that the point must be indivisible?
A) Because it is the smallest possible unit of length.
B) Because otherwise contact would require an infinite regression of divisible surfaces.
C) Because it has no dimensions at all.
D) Because it is defined as clearly indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The existence of physical contact necessitates the existence of indivisible points, which are the ultimate constituents of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every physical contact ultimately rests on an indivisible point, what is the 'indivisible point' in your own life—the core belief or commitment you cannot divide or escape? Are you avoiding confronting it?
?
The argument states that contact with non-existence is impossible. In your daily actions, are you making 'contact' with what is real and true, or are you trying to build on falsehoods that lead to nothing?
?
Consider the infinite regress that would result if there were no stopping point. In your spiritual journey, have you ever reached a point where you must simply affirm 'this is the truth' without further justification? What is holding you back from that affirmation?

The First Proof from Points for Indivisible Parts
The mutakallimun prove the existence of indivisible parts using the point as the extremity of lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun argue for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms) from the geometric concepts of points and lines.

Core Idea
Points are indivisible extremities that prove the existence of indivisible parts in bodies.

The mutakallimun, in their quest to establish atomism, turn to geometry. They observe that when two lines meet, they do so at a point, which is indivisible. This point, being an extremity of an existing line, must itself exist and be indivisible. From this, they argue that bodies must be composed of indivisible parts (atoms).

Guided Path
1
Read the proof of the line and point
Read the Arabic passage that establishes the existence of the line from the contact of two bodies, and then the point as the contact of two lines.
The text is dense; focus on the logical steps.
2
Understand the argument for the indivisible part
The proof moves from the point to an indivisible substance within the body. If it is a substance, it is the sought part; if an accident, it requires an indivisible locus, leading to an indivisible substance.
This is a crucial step in kalam atomism.
3
Reflect on the closure
The argument concludes that all parts of the body are indivisible substances. Consider how this reasoning builds on the earlier proof of the line and point.
This lesson covers the core of the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمرين كما عرفت وذلك هو الخط فثبت وجوده أيضاً. ثم إن الخطين الموجودين يتماسان على أمر ذي وضع لا ينقسم أصلاً وهو النقطة، (وأيضاً فإنها) أي النقطة طرف للخط وهو للسطح وهو للجسم وطرف الموجود موجود فتكون النقطة موجودة (ثم إنها لا تنقسم أصلاً). (قلنا: في الجسم موجود ذو وضع لا ينقسم فإن كان جوهراً فهو المطلوب) لأن ذلك الجوهر الذي لا يقبل الانقسام بوجه من الوجوه جزء للجسم، (وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن جوهراً بل عرضاً (لكان له محل لا ينقسم وإلا انقسم الحال فيه لما مر مراراً) وذلك المحل إن كان جوهراً فذاك وإن كان عرضاً كان له محل آخر (ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى جوهر كذلك أي غير منقسم (وهو الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ). وقد وقع جزءاً للجسم ثم إذا أخرجناه عن الجسم واعتبرنا التماس بالقياس إلى ما كان مجاوراً له وهكذا ظهر أن أجزاءه كلها جواهر غير قابلة للانقسام كما هو مطلوبنا. وقد أجابوا عن ذلك بأن النقطة عرض غير سار في محله فلا يلزم من انقسام محلها انقسامها بل الأطراف كلها أعراض لكن الخط سار في محله في جهة واحدة فينقسم في هذه الجهة فقط والسطح سار في جهتين فينقسم فيهما فقط والنقطة لا سريان لها فلا انقسام فيها. قوله: (وطرف الموجود موجود) لأنه إما جوهر أو عرض قائم به. قوله: (بل الأطراف إلخ) كلمة لترقي بيان فائدة زائدة على المقصود لا للإضراب. قوله: (وقد أجابوا عن ذلك) أي الحكماء فإنهم يزعمون أن انقسام الحال بانقسام المحل مختص بما يكون حلوله سريانياً كالبياض في الجسم. قوله: (غير سار في محله) إذ النقطة مثلاً عارضة للخط من حيث انتهائه في جهة لا حيث هو هو فلا يلزم من انقسامه انقسامها وقس عليها الخط بالنسبة إلى السطح والسطح بالنسبة إلى الجسم التعليمي.
The two things as you know, and that is the line, so its existence is also established. Then the two existing lines are tangent upon something having position that does not divide at all, which is the point. (And also it) i.e., the point is the extremity of the line, and it is of the surface, and it is of the body, and the extremity of an existing thing is existing, so the point exists. (Then it does not divide at all). (We say: In the body there is an existing thing having position that does not divide; if it is a substance, then it is what is sought) because that substance which does not accept division in any way is a part of the body. (And if not) i.e., if it is not a substance but an accident, (then it would have a locus that does not divide, otherwise the accident in it would divide, as has been repeated many times). That locus, if it is a substance, that is it; and if it is an accident, it has another locus. (And there is no infinite regress, rather it ends at a substance similarly i.e., non-divisible) (and it is the part which is not further divisible). And it has become a part of the body; then if we remove it from the body and consider tangency in relation to what was adjacent to it, and so on, it becomes apparent that all its parts are substances not susceptible to division, as is what we seek. And they have answered that by saying that the point is an accident not extending in its locus, so the division of its locus does not necessitate its division; rather the extremities are all accidents, but the line extends in its locus in one direction, so it divides only in that direction, and the surface extends in two directions, so it divides only in them, and the point has no extension, so it does not divide. (His statement: 'And the extremity of an existing thing is existing' because it is either a substance or an accident subsisting in it.) (His statement: 'Rather the extremities etc.') is a word for raising a beneficial explanation additional to the purpose, not for setting aside. (His statement: 'And they have answered that') i.e., the philosophers, for they claim that the division of an accident by the division of its locus is specific to that whose inherence is extensional, like whiteness in the body. (His statement: 'Not extending in its locus') for the point, for example, is accidental to the line in respect of its termination in a direction, not in respect of its essence, so its division does not necessitate its division; and analogize to it the line in relation to the surface and the surface in relation to the instructional body.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the mutakallimun for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms), along with the objection from the philosophers and the commentators' clarification. The proof begins with the established existence of a line formed by the contact of two bodies. Two such lines, existing and tangent, must meet at something having position and being indivisible—this is the point. Since the point is the extremity of the line, and an extremity of an existing thing exists, the point exists and is indivisible. The argument then proceeds: in the body there is something having position that does not divide; if it is a substance, that is the sought indivisible part; if it is an accident, it must inhere in a locus that does not divide (otherwise the accident would divide with the locus, which is impossible). That locus eventually terminates in an indivisible substance, which is 'the part which is not further divisible' (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). Thus all parts of the body are indivisible substances. The philosophers object that the point is an accident not extending in its locus, so division of the line (its locus) does not entail division of the point; only what is extensional (sarī) divides with its locus. The commentator explains that the point is a mere accidental termination, not an inherent quality. He also notes that the phrase 'rather the extremities' is for added benefit, not contradiction.

Key Terms
نقطة
خط
جوهر
عرض
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point is a physical part of the body.
Correction: The point is an extremity, an accident, not a substance. The proof uses the point as evidence to infer an indivisible substance within the body.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what does the existence of an indivisible point imply about the body?
A) The body is infinitely divisible.
B) The body must contain an indivisible substance.
C) The body is composed of points.
D) The point is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The first proof from points establishes a logical necessity for indivisible parts in bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that every part of your body is an indivisible substance sustained by Allah. Do you live as if your body is merely matter, or as a trust from the Creator? When you eat, sleep, or act, do you remember that every atom is dependent on Him? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
The argument avoids infinite regress by ending at a substance. In your life, do you avoid infinite regress in your excuses? Do you keep pushing responsibility back to circumstances, or do you accept that you are a created substance accountable to Allah? What would it take to stop the regress and take ownership today?
?
If the point (an accident) is a proof for what is real and foundational, what 'accidents' in your daily life point you toward the substance of your faith? Do you treat temporary pleasures as if they are the substance, or do they remind you of the eternal reality? How can you let them be pointers, not idols?

The Philosophers' Objection: The Point as a Non-Extensional Accident
The philosophers object that the point does not divide with the line because it is a non-extensional accident, and the mutakallimun respond.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection to the point-based proof and how the mutakallimun defend the argument by distinguishing between extensional and non-extensional accidents.

Core Idea
Points are non-extensional accidents; their indivisibility does not rely on the division of their locus.

The philosophers challenge the mutakallimun's proof by arguing that the point is a mere accident of termination, not an intrinsic quality that extends through the line. Therefore, the point's indivisibility does not prove an indivisible substance in the body. The mutakallimun's response clarifies the distinction between extensional and non-extensional accidents, reinforcing the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the response
Read the Arabic passage where the philosophers argue that the point is an accident not extending in its locus, so it does not divide when the line divides.
The response explains that only extensional accidents divide with their locus.
2
Understand the concept of sariyan (extensional) vs. non-extensional
The text distinguishes between accidents that are sarī (extending) like whiteness, and those that are not, like the point. This is key to the objection.
The commentator also clarifies that 'rather the extremities' is for added benefit.
3
See how the proof remains intact
Despite the objection, the mutakallimun maintain that the point's existence still indicates an indivisible substance because the point is an accident of termination, and its locus (the line) does not need to be indivisible for the point to be indivisible.
This lesson clarifies the philosophical debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمرين كما عرفت وذلك هو الخط فثبت وجوده أيضاً. ثم إن الخطين الموجودين يتماسان على أمر ذي وضع لا ينقسم أصلاً وهو النقطة، (وأيضاً فإنها) أي النقطة طرف للخط وهو للسطح وهو للجسم وطرف الموجود موجود فتكون النقطة موجودة (ثم إنها لا تنقسم أصلاً). (قلنا: في الجسم موجود ذو وضع لا ينقسم فإن كان جوهراً فهو المطلوب) لأن ذلك الجوهر الذي لا يقبل الانقسام بوجه من الوجوه جزء للجسم، (وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن جوهراً بل عرضاً (لكان له محل لا ينقسم وإلا انقسم الحال فيه لما مر مراراً) وذلك المحل إن كان جوهراً فذاك وإن كان عرضاً كان له محل آخر (ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى جوهر كذلك أي غير منقسم (وهو الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ). وقد وقع جزءاً للجسم ثم إذا أخرجناه عن الجسم واعتبرنا التماس بالقياس إلى ما كان مجاوراً له وهكذا ظهر أن أجزاءه كلها جواهر غير قابلة للانقسام كما هو مطلوبنا. وقد أجابوا عن ذلك بأن النقطة عرض غير سار في محله فلا يلزم من انقسام محلها انقسامها بل الأطراف كلها أعراض لكن الخط سار في محله في جهة واحدة فينقسم في هذه الجهة فقط والسطح سار في جهتين فينقسم فيهما فقط والنقطة لا سريان لها فلا انقسام فيها. قوله: (وطرف الموجود موجود) لأنه إما جوهر أو عرض قائم به. قوله: (بل الأطراف إلخ) كلمة لترقي بيان فائدة زائدة على المقصود لا للإضراب. قوله: (وقد أجابوا عن ذلك) أي الحكماء فإنهم يزعمون أن انقسام الحال بانقسام المحل مختص بما يكون حلوله سريانياً كالبياض في الجسم. قوله: (غير سار في محله) إذ النقطة مثلاً عارضة للخط من حيث انتهائه في جهة لا حيث هو هو فلا يلزم من انقسامه انقسامها وقس عليها الخط بالنسبة إلى السطح والسطح بالنسبة إلى الجسم التعليمي.
The two things as you know, and that is the line, so its existence is also established. Then the two existing lines are tangent upon something having position that does not divide at all, which is the point. (And also it) i.e., the point is the extremity of the line, and it is of the surface, and it is of the body, and the extremity of an existing thing is existing, so the point exists. (Then it does not divide at all). (We say: In the body there is an existing thing having position that does not divide; if it is a substance, then it is what is sought) because that substance which does not accept division in any way is a part of the body. (And if not) i.e., if it is not a substance but an accident, (then it would have a locus that does not divide, otherwise the accident in it would divide, as has been repeated many times). That locus, if it is a substance, that is it; and if it is an accident, it has another locus. (And there is no infinite regress, rather it ends at a substance similarly i.e., non-divisible) (and it is the part which is not further divisible). And it has become a part of the body; then if we remove it from the body and consider tangency in relation to what was adjacent to it, and so on, it becomes apparent that all its parts are substances not susceptible to division, as is what we seek. And they have answered that by saying that the point is an accident not extending in its locus, so the division of its locus does not necessitate its division; rather the extremities are all accidents, but the line extends in its locus in one direction, so it divides only in that direction, and the surface extends in two directions, so it divides only in them, and the point has no extension, so it does not divide. (His statement: 'And the extremity of an existing thing is existing' because it is either a substance or an accident subsisting in it.) (His statement: 'Rather the extremities etc.') is a word for raising a beneficial explanation additional to the purpose, not for setting aside. (His statement: 'And they have answered that') i.e., the philosophers, for they claim that the division of an accident by the division of its locus is specific to that whose inherence is extensional, like whiteness in the body. (His statement: 'Not extending in its locus') for the point, for example, is accidental to the line in respect of its termination in a direction, not in respect of its essence, so its division does not necessitate its division; and analogize to it the line in relation to the surface and the surface in relation to the instructional body.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the mutakallimun for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms), along with the objection from the philosophers and the commentators' clarification. The proof begins with the established existence of a line formed by the contact of two bodies. Two such lines, existing and tangent, must meet at something having position and being indivisible—this is the point. Since the point is the extremity of the line, and an extremity of an existing thing exists, the point exists and is indivisible. The argument then proceeds: in the body there is something having position that does not divide; if it is a substance, that is the sought indivisible part; if it is an accident, it must inhere in a locus that does not divide (otherwise the accident would divide with the locus, which is impossible). That locus eventually terminates in an indivisible substance, which is 'the part which is not further divisible' (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). Thus all parts of the body are indivisible substances. The philosophers object that the point is an accident not extending in its locus, so division of the line (its locus) does not entail division of the point; only what is extensional (sarī) divides with its locus. The commentator explains that the point is a mere accidental termination, not an inherent quality. He also notes that the phrase 'rather the extremities' is for added benefit, not contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any accident inhering in a divisible locus must itself divide when the locus divides.
Correction: Only extensional accidents (those that spread throughout the locus) divide with it. Non-extensional accidents, like the point, do not.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' objection, why does the point not divide when the line divides?
A) Because the point is a substance.
B) Because the point is a non-extensional accident.
C) Because the line is indivisible.
D) Because the point is larger than the line.

Key Takeaway
The point's indivisibility does not depend on the line's divisibility because it is a non-extensional accident of termination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your senses to perceive the world, but you have never seen a point or an atom. The philosophers say the point is an accident, not a substance. What evidence do you have for the existence of anything you cannot sense? Does your faith rest on such rational arguments, or on something else? Will you today commit to examining the foundations of your belief in the unseen?
?
This passage says that some accidents do not extend in their locus. You are a locus of many attributes—anger, love, faith. Are any of these attributes 'non-extensional'? Do they define you entirely, or are they accidents that could be removed without destroying you? What is the real you that remains even when your attributes change? Spend this week reflecting on which of your qualities are essential and which are merely accidental.
?
You believe that your body will be resurrected, composed of the very atoms that die. But if atoms are indivisible, how can they be reassembled? Does this passage strengthen or weaken your trust in Allah’s power to resurrect? Do you intellectually accept the theologians’ proof, or does a doubt linger?
