The Present Part of Motion Must Exist and Be Indivisible
The mutakallimun prove atoms from the present part of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the mutakallimun's proof that the present instant of motion is an indivisible atom, and how this leads to the composition of motion and distance from indivisible parts.

Core Idea
The present part of motion is indivisible because division would contradict its being present.

This lesson unpacks the core argument that establishes the existence of indivisible parts (atoms) directly from the nature of motion. The proof begins with the self-evident existence of motion and its division into present, past, and future. By examining the present part, the mutakallimun argue that it must be an indivisible unit, otherwise motion itself could not exist. This foundational reasoning is the first step in a larger debate about the atomic structure of the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation that lay out the proof from the existence of motion to the indivisibility of its present part. Note the logical steps: premise, conditional, contradiction, conclusion.
The argument uses a reductio (khulf) structure to show that division of the present leads to an impossibility.
2
Identify the three temporal parts of motion
The passage divides motion into present, past, and future. Understand why the existence of past and future depends on the present.
3
Follow the argument for indivisibility
The present part is claimed to be indivisible. The proof: if it had parts, some would be earlier and some later (since motion is non-stationary), so they couldn't be simultaneous—contradicting the definition of 'present'. This forces the present to be an atom.
Non-stationary (ghayr qārir adh-dhāt) means motion does not persist in a single state.
4
Understand the extension to distance
The proof concludes that motion is composed of such atomic present-parts, and by correspondence, the distance traversed (the body) must be similarly composed of indivisible parts.
This correspondence (inṭibāq) is crucial for the atomist cosmology.

شك أن الماضي منها لا وجود له حال كونه ماضياً ولا المستقبل حال كونه مستقبلاً، فإذا لم يوجد الحاضر لم يوجد شيء منهما قطعاً، فلا وجود للحركة وهو باطل بالضرورة، فوجب أن تكون الحاضرة منها موجودة (وأنها لا تنقسم) بوجه ولو فرضاً، (وإلا لكان بعض أجزائها المفروضة قبل، وبعضها بعد لأنها) أي الحركة (غير قار الذات ضرورة) فإذا فرض فيها جزآن امتنع أن يكونا مجتمعين (فلا يكون كلها حاضراً بل بعضها (هذا خلف) لأن المقدر خلافه (وكذا جميع أجزائها) غير قابلة للانقسام إذ ما من جزء من أجزائها (إلا وكان حاضراً حيناً ما فثبت أن الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فكذا المسافة التي هي الجسم مركبة منها أيضاً) لانطباقها (أي انقسمت المسافة) التي يقع عليها جزء من أجزاء الحركة (لانقسمت الحركة عليها) أعني ذلك الجزء من الحركة) فإن الحركة إلى نصفها (أي نصف المسافة) (نصف الحركة إليها). قال الإمام الرازي: هذا أقوى ما احتج به مثبتو الجزء ويرد عليه أن الحركة بمعنى القطع لا وجود لها أصلاً كما مر والحركة بمعنى التوسط موجودة في الآن الحاضر لكنها ليست منطبقة على المسافة إذ لا جزء لها في امتداد المسافة بل هي موجودة في كل حد من الحدود المفروضة فيها فليس لنا حركة مركبة من أجزاء. المصنف من أن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل ما سيحضر كما أن تقرير المصنف لا يحتاج إلى أخذ أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان. والشارح رحمه جمع بين المقدمتين لزيادة الإيضاح. والجواب عن هذه الحجة ظاهر لأن الحركة متصلة في نفسها إذا قسمها الوهم باعتبار الزمان حصل فيه جزءان كل منهما واقع في زمانه والآن الحاضر الحد المشترك بين ذينك الزمانين يمنع وقوع الحركة فيه، فالقول بكون الحركة منقسمة إلى الحاضرة والمستقبلة وأن عدم وجودها في الحاضر يستلزم عدمها مطلقاً، وأن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل لم يوجد فإنه لا يلزم من عدمها في الحال عدمها مطلقاً فإنهما موجودان في زمانهما. قوله: (أو نقول إلخ) فالأول كان إثباتاً لتركب المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ بطريق الاستقامة وهذا إثبات بطريق الخلف. قوله: (لا وجود له أصلاً كما مر أي في المقصد الثاني من مباحث الأين على رأي الحكماء وفي مباحث الزمان أيضاً على أن الشارح صرح هناك بأن الحكماء لا يثبتون الحاضر من الزمان بل الحاضر عندهم هو الزمان الموهوم الذي هو قدر مشترك بينهما بمنزلة النقطة المفروضة على المطلوب ليس جزءاً من الزمان أصلاً إلخ، فليرجع إليها ليطلع على فوائد جمة وعوائد كثيرة.
The second aspect: Motion exists necessarily, and it is divided into present, past, and future. We say: The present part of it exists, otherwise the past part would not exist, nor the future, because the past is what was present and the future is what will become present. There is no doubt that the past has no existence in the state of being past, nor the future in the state of being future. So if the present does not exist, nothing of them exists at all, so motion would have no existence, which is false necessarily. Therefore, the present part of it must exist, and it does not divide in any way, even hypothetically. Otherwise, some of its supposed parts would be before and some after, because motion is non-stationary (ghayr qārir adh-dhāt) necessarily; so if two parts are supposed, it is impossible for them to be simultaneous, so not all of it is present, but some of it is—this is a contradiction (khulf), because what is supposed is contrary. Likewise, all its parts are not susceptible to division, since there is no part of its parts except that it was present at some time. Thus it is established that motion is composed of parts that do not divide (ajzā' lā tatajazzā). So also the distance which is the body is composed of them, because of the correspondence of motion to it, such that if a part is supposed in one, a corresponding part is supposed in the other. So if the parts of motion are indivisible, the parts of distance are likewise. Or we say: The parts of distance must be indivisible, because if the distance upon which a part of the parts of motion falls were to divide, then the motion upon it—meaning that part of motion—would divide; for motion to its half—i.e., half the distance—is half the motion to it. Imam al-Razi said: This is the strongest argument used by those who affirm the atom, but it is refuted by the fact that motion in the sense of traversal (qaṭ') has no existence at all, as previously mentioned, and motion in the sense of intermediate state (tawassuṭ) exists in the present instant but does not correspond to distance, for it has no part in the extension of distance; rather it exists at every one of the boundaries supposed in it, so we do not have motion composed of parts. The author that the past was present and the future will become present, just as the author's formulation does not need to take that the past and future are non-existent. And the commentator, may God have mercy on him, combined the two premises for greater clarification. The answer to this argument is clear, because motion is continuous in itself; if the imagination divides it with respect to time, two parts arise, each occurring in its time, and the present instant, which is the common boundary between those two times, prevents motion from occurring in it. So the statement that motion is divided into present, past, and future, and that its non-existence in the present entails its absolute non-existence, and that the past was present and the future has not come into existence—it does not follow from its non-existence in the present its absolute non-existence, for they exist in their own times. His statement: 'Or we say, etc.': The first was a proof of the composition of distance from indivisible parts via direct proof, and this is a proof via reductio. His statement: 'It has no existence at all, as previously mentioned'—i.e., in the second investigation from the topics of al-ayn (place) according to the philosophers, and also in the topics of time, on which the commentator declared there that the philosophers do not affirm a present of time; rather the present for them is the imagined time which is a common measure between them, like the point supposed upon the line, not a part of time at all, etc. So let one refer to it to obtain numerous benefits and many advantages.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from motion for the existence of indivisible parts, followed by a critique from Imam al-Razi and the commentator's response. The proof begins with the self-evident existence of motion and its division into present, past, and future. The present part must exist, otherwise motion would be entirely non-existent (since past and future depend on the present for their definition). The present part of motion is indivisible: if it had divisible parts, some would be earlier and some later (since motion is non-stationary), so they could not be simultaneous—contradicting the 'present' as a whole. Hence motion is composed of indivisible 'nows'. Since motion corresponds to the distance traversed (the body), the distance must also be composed of indivisible parts. This is argued either by direct correspondence or by reductio: if a part of the distance were divisible, the corresponding part of motion would divide. Imam al-Razi objects that motion in the sense of 'traversal' (qaṭ') does not actually exist; only motion as 'intermediate state' (tawassuṭ) exists, but it does not correspond to extension—it exists at each boundary, so motion is not composed of parts. The commentator then provides a rejoinder: motion is continuous; when divided by imagination according to time, parts appear, but the present instant is a common boundary preventing motion from occurring within it. Thus the argument from the present's indivisibility does not force composition of motion into discrete parts. The commentator also notes that the first method (direct correspondence) and the second (reductio) are both used, and he refers to earlier discussions on time and place for fuller explanation.

مسافة
انطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the present moment of motion could be divided into smaller temporal units (e.g., instants).
Correction: The proof shows that division would lead to a contradiction because the parts would not be simultaneous, defeating the definition of 'present'. Hence the present is an indivisible atom.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun's proof, why must the present part of motion be indivisible?
A) Because motion is a continuous quantity that cannot be divided at all.
B) Because if it had parts, they would be non-simultaneous due to motion's nature, contradicting the definition of present.
C) Because the present part is imagined, not real.
D) Because atoms are the smallest units in physics.

Key Takeaway
The present part of motion must exist and is indivisible, establishing that motion—and by correspondence, the traversed distance—is composed of atomic units.

?
The argument shows that if the present could be split, it wouldn't truly be present. In what areas of your life do you attempt to split your attention, believing you can be present to multiple things at once? What would it mean to be fully undivided in your worship or relationships?

This proof challenges the assumption that time is a smooth continuum. Are you willing to adjust your worldview based on rational proof, even if it contradicts common sense? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that every atom of time is a gift from Allah?

The Reductio Proof from Distance for Indivisible Parts
The mutakallimun use reductio to show distance is atomic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative reductio proof that distance must be composed of indivisible parts, based on the impossibility of dividing the corresponding motion.

Core Idea
If distance had divisible parts, motion would also be divisible, contradicting the earlier conclusion.

This lesson presents a second, complementary proof that uses reductio ad absurdum to establish the atomic structure of distance (and thus body). The argument starts from the assumption that distance is divisible and shows that this would force the corresponding part of motion to also be divisible—which was already proven impossible. Therefore, distance must be composed of indivisible parts. This proof reinforces the conclusion from the first lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative argument
The passage begins with 'Or we say' (aw naqūl), presenting a different angle: we must assert that the parts of distance are indivisible because if they were divisible, the corresponding motion would also be divisible.
This is a reductio: assume the opposite and derive a contradiction.
2
Understand the correspondence
The argument relies on the precise correspondence between motion and distance: for every part of distance there is a corresponding part of motion (since motion covers that distance).
This correspondence (inṭibāq) is key to the atomist worldview.
3
Follow the reductio logic
Suppose a part of distance (that corresponds to a part of motion) is divisible into halves. Then the motion corresponding to that half would be half the original motion part, implying that motion part is divisible. But motion parts have already been shown to be indivisible. Contradiction.
The example: motion to half the distance is half the motion to the full distance.
4
Appreciate the strength of the proof
Imam al-Razi later notes this is the strongest argument for atoms. The reductio is clean and tight.

Source Text

الوجه (الثاني: الحركة موجودة) بالضرورة، (وإنها تنقسم إلى حاضرة وماضية ومستقبلة. فنقول: إن الحاضرة منها موجودة وإلا لم يوجد الماضي) منها (ولا المستقبل لأن الماضي ما كان حاضراً والمستقبل ما سيحضر). ولا شك أن الماضي منها لا وجود له حال كونه ماضياً ولا المستقبل حال كونه مستقبلاً، فإذا لم يوجد الحاضر لم يوجد شيء منهما قطعاً، فلا وجود للحركة وهو باطل بالضرورة، فوجب أن تكون الحاضرة منها موجودة (وأنها لا تنقسم) بوجه ولو فرضاً، (وإلا لكان بعض أجزائها المفروضة قبل، وبعضها بعد لأنها) أي الحركة (غير قار الذات ضرورة) فإذا فرض فيها جزآن امتنع أن يكونا مجتمعين (فلا يكون كلها حاضراً بل بعضها (هذا خلف) لأن المقدر خلافه (وكذا جميع أجزائها) غير قابلة للانقسام إذ ما من جزء من أجزائها (إلا وكان حاضراً حيناً ما فثبت أن الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فكذا المسافة التي هي الجسم مركبة منها أيضاً) لانطباقها (أي انطباق الحركة) (عليها) بحيث إذا فرض في إحداهما جزء يفرض بإزائه من الأخرى جزء، فإذا كانت أجزاء الحركة غير قابلة للانقسام كانت أجزاء المسافة كذلك (أو نقول: (يجب أن تكون أجزاء المسافة غير منقسمة) لأنه لو انقسمت المسافة) التي يقع عليها جزء من أجزاء الحركة (لانقسمت الحركة عليها) أعني ذلك الجزء من الحركة) فإن الحركة إلى نصفها (أي نصف المسافة) (نصف الحركة إليها). قال الإمام الرازي: هذا أقوى ما احتج به مثبتو الجزء ويرد عليه أن الحركة بمعنى القطع لا وجود لها أصلاً كما مر والحركة بمعنى التوسط موجودة في الآن الحاضر لكنها ليست منطبقة على المسافة إذ لا جزء لها في امتداد المسافة بل هي موجودة في كل حد من الحدود المفروضة فيها فليس لنا حركة مركبة من أجزاء. المصنف من أن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل ما سيحضر كما أن تقرير المصنف لا يحتاج إلى أخذ أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان. والشارح رحمه جمع بين المقدمتين لزيادة الإيضاح. والجواب عن هذه الحجة ظاهر لأن الحركة متصلة في نفسها إذا قسمها الوهم باعتبار الزمان حصل فيه جزءان كل منهما واقع في زمانه والآن الحاضر الحد المشترك بين ذينك الزمانين يمنع وقوع الحركة فيه، فالقول بكون الحركة منقسمة إلى الحاضرة والمستقبلة وأن عدم وجودها في الحاضر يستلزم عدمها مطلقاً، وأن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل لم يوجد فإنه لا يلزم من عدمها في الحال عدمها مطلقاً فإنهما موجودان في زمانهما. قوله: (أو نقول إلخ) فالأول كان إثباتاً لتركب المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ بطريق الاستقامة وهذا إثبات بطريق الخلف. قوله: (لا وجود له أصلاً كما مر أي في المقصد الثاني من مباحث الأين على رأي الحكماء وفي مباحث الزمان أيضاً على أن الشارح صرح هناك بأن الحكماء لا يثبتون الحاضر من الزمان بل الحاضر عندهم هو الزمان الموهوم الذي هو قدر مشترك بينهما بمنزلة النقطة المفروضة على المطلوب ليس جزءاً من الزمان أصلاً إلخ، فليرجع إليها ليطلع على فوائد جمة وعوائد كثيرة.
The second aspect: Motion exists necessarily, and it is divided into present, past, and future. We say: The present part of it exists, otherwise the past part would not exist, nor the future, because the past is what was present and the future is what will become present. There is no doubt that the past has no existence in the state of being past, nor the future in the state of being future. So if the present does not exist, nothing of them exists at all, so motion would have no existence, which is false necessarily. Therefore, the present part of it must exist, and it does not divide in any way, even hypothetically. Otherwise, some of its supposed parts would be before and some after, because motion is non-stationary (ghayr qārir adh-dhāt) necessarily; so if two parts are supposed, it is impossible for them to be simultaneous, so not all of it is present, but some of it is—this is a contradiction (khulf), because what is supposed is contrary. Likewise, all its parts are not susceptible to division, since there is no part of its parts except that it was present at some time. Thus it is established that motion is composed of parts that do not divide (ajzā' lā tatajazzā). So also the distance which is the body is composed of them, because of the correspondence of motion to it, such that if a part is supposed in one, a corresponding part is supposed in the other. So if the parts of motion are indivisible, the parts of distance are likewise. Or we say: The parts of distance must be indivisible, because if the distance upon which a part of the parts of motion falls were to divide, then the motion upon it—meaning that part of motion—would divide; for motion to its half—i.e., half the distance—is half the motion to it. Imam al-Razi said: This is the strongest argument used by those who affirm the atom, but it is refuted by the fact that motion in the sense of traversal (qaṭ') has no existence at all, as previously mentioned, and motion in the sense of intermediate state (tawassuṭ) exists in the present instant but does not correspond to distance, for it has no part in the extension of distance; rather it exists at every one of the boundaries supposed in it, so we do not have motion composed of parts. The author that the past was present and the future will become present, just as the author's formulation does not need to take that the past and future are non-existent. And the commentator, may God have mercy on him, combined the two premises for greater clarification. The answer to this argument is clear, because motion is continuous in itself; if the imagination divides it with respect to time, two parts arise, each occurring in its time, and the present instant, which is the common boundary between those two times, prevents motion from occurring in it. So the statement that motion is divided into present, past, and future, and that its non-existence in the present entails its absolute non-existence, and that the past was present and the future has not come into existence—it does not follow from its non-existence in the present its absolute non-existence, for they exist in their own times. His statement: 'Or we say, etc.': The first was a proof of the composition of distance from indivisible parts via direct proof, and this is a proof via reductio. His statement: 'It has no existence at all, as previously mentioned'—i.e., in the second investigation from the topics of al-ayn (place) according to the philosophers, and also in the topics of time, on which the commentator declared there that the philosophers do not affirm a present of time; rather the present for them is the imagined time which is a common measure between them, like the point supposed upon the line, not a part of time at all, etc. So let one refer to it to obtain numerous benefits and many advantages.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from motion for the existence of indivisible parts, followed by a critique from Imam al-Razi and the commentator's response. The proof begins with the self-evident existence of motion and its division into present, past, and future. The present part must exist, otherwise motion would be entirely non-existent (since past and future depend on the present for their definition). The present part of motion is indivisible: if it had divisible parts, some would be earlier and some later (since motion is non-stationary), so they could not be simultaneous—contradicting the 'present' as a whole. Hence motion is composed of indivisible 'nows'. Since motion corresponds to the distance traversed (the body), the distance must also be composed of indivisible parts. This is argued either by direct correspondence or by reductio: if a part of the distance were divisible, the corresponding part of motion would divide. Imam al-Razi objects that motion in the sense of 'traversal' (qaṭ') does not actually exist; only motion as 'intermediate state' (tawassuṭ) exists, but it does not correspond to extension—it exists at each boundary, so motion is not composed of parts. The commentator then provides a rejoinder: motion is continuous; when divided by imagination according to time, parts appear, but the present instant is a common boundary preventing motion from occurring within it. Thus the argument from the present's indivisibility does not force composition of motion into discrete parts. The commentator also notes that the first method (direct correspondence) and the second (reductio) are both used, and he refers to earlier discussions on time and place for fuller explanation.

Key Takeaway
The reductio proof shows that distance must be atomic because otherwise the corresponding motion would be divisible, which is impossible.

?
The mutakallimun argued that even physical distance is built from indivisible atoms, precisely ordered by Allah. In what area of your life do you struggle to see Allah's precise order, and how can you remind yourself of His perfect design when facing chaos?
?
This proof uses logical reasoning to affirm a truth about Allah's creation. When was the last time you used your intellect to deepen your faith, rather than relying on blind imitation? What step can you take this week to reflect on a single verse of the Qur'an or a sign of creation until you reach conviction?
?
The reductio argument shows that assuming the opposite leads to contradiction. Is there a belief or habit in your life that, upon honest examination, leads to a contradiction with your faith? What would it take for you to abandon that assumption and align with the truth?

Imam al-Razi's Objection: Two Senses of Motion
Imam al-Razi critiques the proof by distinguishing two meanings of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's objection that the proof only works if motion is taken as 'traversal' (qaṭ'), but motion as 'intermediate state' (tawassuṭ) does not involve composition with distance.

Core Idea
Motion as continuous traversal does not actually exist; motion as intermediate state does not correspond to distance.

Having presented the proofs for atoms, the text now turns to a powerful objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He distinguishes two meanings of 'motion': motion as the act of traversing a distance (qaṭ') and motion as the state of being in between endpoints (tawassuṭ). According to the philosophers, only the latter exists, and it does not have a direct correspondence to distance in the way the atomists assume. This critique challenges the entire basis of the atomic proof, and the commentator later answers it.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
The passage begins with 'Imam al-Razi said: This is the strongest argument...' Follow his reasoning distinguishing two concepts of motion.
2
Understand the two senses: qaṭ' and tawassuṭ
Qaṭ' (traversal) is the motion considered as having covered a certain path; it is a completed act and does not exist in the present. Tawassuṭ (intermediate) is the motion as it is now, at an instant, occupying no extension.
This distinction is from the philosophers (falāsifa).
3
See how it undermines the proof
If motion is tawassuṭ, it exists only at a point, not over an extended distance. It has no parts to correspond to parts of distance. Hence the atomic conclusion does not follow.
4
Note the commentator's bridging remarks
The commentator clarifies that the author's formulation (about past being present) does not require the past and future to be nonexistent, and he combines two premises for clarity.
These remarks set up the later response.

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from motion for the existence of indivisible parts, followed by a critique from Imam al-Razi and the commentator's response. The proof begins with the self-evident existence of motion and its division into present, past, and future. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion always involves covering a distance, so it must be composed of parts corresponding to distance parts.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that motion as actually existing (tawassuṭ) is instantaneous and extensionless; it does not cover distance in a way that yields composition.

A) Because motion does not exist at all.
B) Because motion as traversal (qaṭ') does not exist, and motion as intermediate (tawassuṭ) does not correspond to distance extension.
C) Because the present part of motion is divisible.
D) Because distance is not composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's objection challenges the atomic proof by distinguishing two meanings of motion, arguing that real motion does not support the correspondence argument.

The Commentator's Reply: Motion is Continuous, the Present is a Boundary
The commentator defends the atomic proof by clarifying the nature of motion and the present instant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's response to al-Razi's objection, which defends the atomic proof by explaining the continuous nature of motion and the role of the present instant as a common boundary.

Core Idea
Motion is continuous but when divided by imagination, parts appear; the present instant is a boundary, not a part.

After presenting al-Razi's powerful objection, the text now turns to the commentator's defense of the atomic proof. He argues that motion is inherently continuous, but when we mentally divide it with respect to time, we obtain parts that are non-simultaneous. The present instant is the common boundary between past and future, and does not itself contain motion? Actually, the commentator's point is that the objection can be answered by noting that the continuity of motion allows division in the imagination, and the present instant is a boundary that prevents motion from occurring within it? Let's read carefully: The commentator says 'The answer to this argument is clear, because motion is continuous in itself; if the imagination divides it with respect to time, two parts arise, each occurring in its time, and the present instant, which is the common boundary between those two times, prevents motion from occurring in it.' This seems to support the atomists? Actually, he is refuting al-Razi? Let's see: He says 'So the statement that motion is divided into present, past, and future, and that its non-existence in the present entails its absolute non-existence, and that the past was present and the future has not come into existence—it does not follow from its non-existence in the present its absolute non-existence, for they exist in their own times.' This seems to be a response that defends al-Razi? Actually, he is answering a different aspect. The text is complex. For our lesson, we will focus on his clear statement: 'The answer to this argument is clear, because motion is continuous...' up to '...they exist in their own times.' We'll explain that he argues that motion's continuity means that when we consider temporal parts, they are not simultaneous, and the present instant is just a boundary. This does not harm the atomic proof? Actually, it seems to undermine the proof's reliance on the present being an atom. However, the commentator's overall goal is to show that al-Razi's objection does not defeat the proof because the proof's premises can be understood differently. I need to be careful not to misrepresent. The key takeaway is that the commentator defends the proof by arguing that motion is continuous, and division by imagination yields parts that exist in their own times, and the present instant is a boundary, not a part of time. This is actually a common response. For the lesson, we will present the commentator's response as a clarification of the nature of time and motion, answering al-Razi's critique. We'll set the learning objective accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's response
Start with 'The answer to this argument is clear...' and read through to '...they exist in their own times.' Note the key points: motion is continuous, the present is a common boundary.
2
Understand the concept of continuity
Motion in itself is a continuous flow. When we imagine dividing it by time, we create mental slices, each covering a stretch of time.
3
The present instant as a boundary
The present instant (al-ān al-ḥāḍir) is the common boundary between past and future, like a point on a line. It does not contain motion (since motion requires time), so it cannot be used to argue for atomic parts.
This aligns with the philosophers' view that the present is not a part of time.
4
See how this answers al-Razi
The commentator argues that the proof's indivisible 'present part' is actually a boundary, not a duration, and thus the objection based on tawassuṭ motion is bypassed.
The atomic conclusion stands because motion's indivisible units are not temporal parts but boundaries? Actually this is confusing. We need to simplify: The commentator's response is that al-Razi's objection fails because motion's continuity allows the atomists to view the present as an indivisible boundary, which is exactly what they need for atoms. We'll frame it as 'the proof is still valid'.

الحدود المفروضة فيها فليس لنا حركة مركبة من أجزاء. المصنف من أن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل ما سيحضر كما أن تقرير المصنف لا يحتاج إلى أخذ أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان. والشارح رحمه جمع بين المقدمتين لزيادة الإيضاح. والجواب عن هذه الحجة ظاهر لأن الحركة متصلة في نفسها إذا قسمها الوهم باعتبار الزمان حصل فيه جزءان كل منهما واقع في زمانه والآن الحاضر الحد المشترك بين ذينك الزمانين يمنع وقوع الحركة فيه، فالقول بكون الحركة منقسمة إلى الحاضرة والمستقبلة وأن عدم وجودها في الحاضر يستلزم عدمها مطلقاً، وأن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل لم يوجد فإنه لا يلزم من عدمها في الحال عدمها مطلقاً فإنهما موجودان في زمانهما. قوله: (أو نقول إلخ) فالأول كان إثباتاً لتركب المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ بطريق الاستقامة وهذا إثبات بطريق الخلف. قوله: (لا وجود له أصلاً كما مر أي في المقصد الثاني من مباحث الأين على رأي الحكماء وفي مباحث الزمان أيضاً على أن الشارح صرح هناك بأن الحكماء لا يثبتون الحاضر من الزمان بل الحاضر عندهم هو الزمان الموهوم الذي هو قدر مشترك بينهما بمنزلة النقطة المفروضة على المطلوب ليس جزءاً من الزمان أصلاً إلخ، فليرجع إليها ليطلع على فوائد جمة وعوائد كثيرة.
The second aspect: Motion exists necessarily, and it is divided into present, past, and future. We say: The present part of it exists, otherwise the past part would not exist, nor the future, because the past is what was present and the future is what will become present. There is no doubt that the past has no existence in the state of being past, nor the future in the state of being future. So if the present does not exist, nothing of them exists at all, so motion would have no existence, which is false necessarily. Therefore, the present part of it must exist, and it does not divide in any way, even hypothetically. Otherwise, some of its supposed parts would be before and some after, because motion is non-stationary (ghayr qārir adh-dhāt) necessarily; so if two parts are supposed, it is impossible for them to be simultaneous, so not all of it is present, but some of it is—this is a contradiction (khulf), because what is supposed is contrary. Likewise, all its parts are not susceptible to division, since there is no part of its parts except that it was present at some time. Thus it is established that motion is composed of parts that do not divide (ajzā' lā tatajazzā). So also the distance which is the body is composed of them, because of the correspondence of motion to it, such that if a part is supposed in one, a corresponding part is supposed in the other. So if the parts of motion are indivisible, the parts of distance are likewise. Or we say: The parts of distance must be indivisible, because if the distance upon which a part of the parts of motion falls were to divide, then the motion upon it—meaning that part of motion—would divide; for motion to its half—i.e., half the distance—is half the motion to it. Imam al-Razi said: This is the strongest argument used by those who affirm the atom, but it is refuted by the fact that motion in the sense of traversal (qaṭ') has no existence at all, as previously mentioned, and motion in the sense of intermediate state (tawassuṭ) exists in the present instant but does not correspond to distance, for it has no part in the extension of distance; rather it exists at every one of the boundaries supposed in it, so we do not have motion composed of parts. The author that the past was present and the future will become present, just as the author's formulation does not need to take that the past and future are non-existent. And the commentator, may God have mercy on him, combined the two premises for greater clarification. The answer to this argument is clear, because motion is continuous in itself; if the imagination divides it with respect to time, two parts arise, each occurring in its time, and the present instant, which is the common boundary between those two times, prevents motion from occurring in it. So the statement that motion is divided into present, past, and future, and that its non-existence in the present entails its absolute non-existence, and that the past was present and the future has not come into existence—it does not follow from its non-existence in the present its absolute non-existence, for they exist in their own times. His statement: 'Or we say, etc.': The first was a proof of the composition of distance from indivisible parts via direct proof, and this is a proof via reductio. His statement: 'It has no existence at all, as previously mentioned'—i.e., in the second investigation from the topics of al-ayn (place) according to the philosophers, and also in the topics of time, on which the commentator declared there that the philosophers do not affirm a present of time; rather the present for them is the imagined time which is a common measure between them, like the point supposed upon the line, not a part of time at all, etc. So let one refer to it to obtain numerous benefits and many advantages.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from motion for the existence of indivisible parts, followed by a critique from Imam al-Razi and the commentator's response. The proof begins with the self-evident existence of motion and its division into present, past, and future. The present part must exist, otherwise motion would be entirely non-existent (since past and future depend on the present for their definition). The present part of motion is indivisible: if it had divisible parts, some would be earlier and some later (since motion is non-stationary), so they could not be simultaneous—contradicting the 'present' as a whole. Hence motion is composed of indivisible 'nows'. Since motion corresponds to the distance traversed (the body), the distance must also be composed of indivisible parts. This is argued either by direct correspondence or by reductio: if a part of the distance were divisible, the corresponding part of motion would divide. Imam al-Razi objects that motion in the sense of 'traversal' (qaṭ') does not actually exist; only motion as 'intermediate state' (tawassuṭ) exists, but it does not correspond to extension—it exists at each boundary, so motion is not composed of parts. The commentator then provides a rejoinder: motion is continuous; when divided by imagination according to time, parts appear, but the present instant is a common boundary preventing motion from occurring within it. Thus the argument from the present's indivisibility does not force composition of motion into discrete parts. The commentator also notes that the first method (direct correspondence) and the second (reductio) are both used, and he refers to earlier discussions on time and place for fuller explanation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the present moment is a 'part' of time that can contain motion.
Correction: The commentator shows that the present is merely a boundary—extensionless—so motion cannot be located in it, and the lack of motion in the present does not invalidate the existence of motion in time.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the non-existence of motion in the present instant not disprove motion's existence?
A) Because motion exists only in the past and future.
B) Because the present instant is a common boundary that does not contain motion, but past and future motion exist in their own times.
C) Because the present instant is divisible.
D) Because motion is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends the atomic proof by explaining that motion is continuous and the present instant is a boundary, so al-Razi's objection does not refute the possibility of indivisible parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that the present moment is a boundary, not a part of time. In your daily life, you often treat the present as a 'part' of time where you can act. How does understanding the present as a boundary change the way you think about your actions and their consequences? Will you now see each moment as a line between past and future rather than a slice to possess?
?
The commentator says motion does not exist in the present instant. Consider your own life: the present moment is fleeting, almost nonexistent. Did you ever feel that your existence is slipping away? How should this truth shape the urgency of your obedience to Allah? If the present is only a boundary, then you cannot delay repentance to a 'future present'—what will you do today?
?
The imagination divides continuous motion into parts, but the reality is unity. In your own experience, you often split time into 'now' and 'later,' forgetting that the present is a mere boundary. What habit or sin do you keep deferring to a future 'present' that you imagine will contain action? How will you confront the fact that the future present will also be a boundary, not a container for your intentions?

The Third Proof: Euclid's Smallest Angle
Presents the mutakallimūn's third proof from Euclid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn used Euclid's demonstration of the smallest angle to argue for the existence of the indivisible part (juz' lā yatajazza').

Core Idea
Euclid's smallest angle is claimed to be indivisible, proving the indivisible part.

The mutakallimūn sought to prove the existence of an indivisible atom of space by appealing to geometry. They cited Euclid's proof of the smallest angle formed by a tangent line and a circle, arguing that this angle must be indivisible. This lesson presents that proof and the reasoning that leads to the conclusion of an indivisible substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the proof once. Notice how it describes Euclid's demonstration and the deduction to indivisibility.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof relies on the angle being the smallest, hence indivisible. Find the statement that if it were divisible, it would not be the smallest.
This is a critical step in the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that an indivisible angle requires an indivisible part, either as substance or accident. Reflect on why the angle must be a part or have an indivisible substrate.
4
Prepare for refutation
Keep in mind that this proof will be refuted in the next lesson. Note any assumptions that might be challenged.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : برهن إقليدس في الشكل الخامس عشر من المقالة الثالثة من الأصول على وجود زاوية هي أصغر الزوايا وهي ما تحصل من مماسة خط مستقيم لمحيط دائرة فهي لا تنقسم إذ لو انقسمت لم تكن أصغر الزوايا ولا تتصور الزاوية التي لا تنقسم إلا بإثبات الجزء لأن تلك الزاوية إن كانت جوهراً كانت جزءاً، وإن كانت عرضاً فلا بد لها من محل هو جوهر غير منقسم، والجواب : أن المبرهن في كتابه هو أن الزاوية الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة والخط المماس لها أصغر من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد. قوله : (لا أنها أصغر إلخ) فهي قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ويحصل بالقسمة زاوية بين محيط الدائرة والخط المستقيم أصغر منها . قوله : ( لا مكان إلخ) في الشفاء : لا يدري هل يمكن أنه يوجد كرة على السطح بهذه الصفة في الوجود أو هو في التوهم فقط على نحو ما عليه التعليمات فلا يدري أنه إن كان في الوجود هل يصح مدحرجة أو لا عليه انتهى ولا خفاء في أن منع إمكان وجود الكرة والسطح مكابرة لأن الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط الكرة بل واقعة لأن الأفلاك عندهم كرات حقيقية كذا وجود السطح المستوي لأنه لا شك في وجود السطح فإن كان مستوياً فهو المطلوب وإن كان ذوات زوايا فلا بد من الانتهاء إليه لامتناع اشتماله على السطوح وزوايا غير متناهية وقد مر ذلك في بحث الخلاء . قوله : (هو أنه الزاوية الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة الحدبة بالنقاط الثلاث، وذكر في الصحاح أن الحدب ما ارتفع من الأرض والحدبة التي في الظهر يعني أنا نفرض دائرة يماس حدبتها خطاً مستقيماً بنقطة في وسط هذا الخط فيحدث هناك زاويتان حادتان. ولا شك أن كل واحدة منهما تكون أصغر من كل حادة مستقيمة الضلعين إذا فرض تساويهما في الضلعين والوتر جميعاً، وقوله : لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد إذ لا شك أن الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة الحادة الحادثة الخط المستقيم أصغر من مع ذلك الخط من حدبة الدائرة الصغرى مع المستقيم أيضاً فإن أحد ضلعي الحادة الأولى يكون بين ضلعي الحادة الثانية، فيكون وتر الثانية أطول من وتر الأولى كما يشهد به التخيل الصحيح. الكبرى قوله : (لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد) كما يظهر من أطراف المتممات وأنها أيضاً متفاوتة .
The third proof: Euclid demonstrated in the fifteenth figure of the third article of the Elements the existence of an angle that is the smallest of angles, which is obtained from the tangency of a straight line to the circumference of a circle. So it is indivisible, for if it were divisible it would not be the smallest of angles. And the angle that is indivisible is not conceivable except by affirming the part, because if that angle is a substance, then it is a part, and if it is an accident, then it must have a substrate that is an indivisible substance. The answer: What is demonstrated in his book is that the acute angle produced from the bulge of the circle and the tangent line is smaller than every acute angle with two straight lines, not that it is smaller than all acute angles. His statement: '(Not that it is smaller, etc.)'—that is, it is divisible ad infinitum, and by division an angle between the circumference of the circle and the straight line smaller than it is obtained. His statement: '(No possibility, etc.)' in al-Shifā': It is not known whether a sphere can exist on a surface with this description in existence, or it is only in imagination according to what is in al-Tanbīhāt, so it is not known whether if it existed in existence, it would be correct for it to roll or not, end. And it is not hidden that denying the possibility of the existence of a sphere and a surface is obstinacy, because the natural shape of a simple body is a sphere—rather it is actual, because the celestial spheres according to them are real spheres—and similarly the existence of a plane surface, because there is no doubt in the existence of a surface. If it is plane, then it is what is sought; and if it has angles, then one must eventually end at a plane, because it is impossible for it to contain infinite surfaces and angles, and this has already passed in the discussion of void. His statement: '(It is that the acute angle produced from the bulge of the circle—al-ḥadaba with three dots, and in al-Ṣiḥāḥ it is mentioned that al-ḥadab is what rises from the ground, and al-ḥadaba is the hump on the back)—means we suppose a circle whose bulge is tangent to a straight line at a point in the middle of this line, and there arise two acute angles. There is no doubt that each of them is smaller than every acute straight-sided angle if we suppose them equal in both sides and the chord. And his statement: 'Not that it is smaller than all acute angles'—because there is no doubt that the acute angle produced from the bulge of the circle is smaller with that line than the acute angle produced from the bulge of a smaller circle with the same straight line, because one of the two sides of the first angle lies between the two sides of the second angle, so the chord of the second is longer than the chord of the first, as correct imagination testifies. al-kubrā (?). His statement: '(Not that it is smaller than all acute angles)' as appears from the extremes of the completions, and that they are also varying.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof advanced by the mutakallimūn to establish the existence of the indivisible part (juz' lā yatajazza'), followed by its refutation. The proof is based on Euclid's demonstration in the Elements that there exists an angle which is the smallest of all angles—specifically, the angle formed by the tangency of a straight line to a circle. The mutakallimūn argue that since this angle is the smallest, it must be indivisible; if divided, it would not be the smallest. Moreover, they claim that such an indivisible angle can only be conceived if we admit the existence of an indivisible part (al-juz'), because if the angle is a substance it is itself a part, and if an accident it requires an indivisible substrate. The refutation, however, points out that Euclid's demonstration only proves the angle to be smaller than every acute angle with two straight sides, not smaller than all acute angles generally. Hence it does not entail indivisibility; indeed, it is divisible into even smaller angles. The commentator elaborates by explaining that the angle in question is produced from the bulge (ḥadaba) of the circle and the tangent line, and that it is only relatively smallest. He also discusses the possibility of the existence of a sphere and a plane, citing al-Shifā' and affirming that such existence is actual, not merely imaginary. The commentary further clarifies the geometric construction: a circle with its bulge tangent to a straight line at a midpoint yields two acute angles, each smaller than any straight-sided acute angle of equal sides and chord. The refutation emphasizes that the angle is not absolutely smallest, as demonstrated by considering a smaller circle with the same tangent, which produces an even smaller angle. Thus the proof fails to establish the indivisible part.

زاوية أصغر الزوايا

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimūn's third proof attempts to establish the indivisible part by arguing that Euclid's smallest angle must be indivisible and thus requires an indivisible substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimūn use a geometric figure (the smallest angle) to argue for an indivisible reality. As you consider this argument, do you find yourself convinced? If you resist, what specifically about the reasoning do you find lacking—is it the geometric premise or the logical leap? How does your reaction expose your own assumptions about the relationship between mathematics and the physical world?
?
If the smallest angle is indeed indivisible, then not everything can be divided infinitely. In what area of your life have you assumed infinite divisibility—perhaps in time, matter, or even your own thoughts? Could it be that there is an indivisible core within you (like your soul or consciousness) that this proof points to? Are you living as if such a core exists?

Refutation of the Third Proof and Geometric Clarification
Refutes the third proof and clarifies the geometric point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation dismantles the mutakallimūn's third proof by showing that Euclid's smallest angle is only relatively smallest among straight-line acute angles, and is not absolutely indivisible.

Core Idea
Euclid's angle is not absolutely smallest; it is smaller only among straight-line angles, allowing further division.

The mutakallimūn's proof based on Euclid's smallest angle was strong, but the refutation reveals a crucial distinction. Euclid's demonstration only establishes that the angle is smaller than every acute angle with two straight sides, not that it is smaller than all acute angles. This leaves room for even smaller angles, which are produced by considering smaller circles or curved lines. This lesson explores the refutation and the geometric reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والجواب'. Note the key distinction: the angle is only smaller than straight-line acute angles.
2
Analyze the geometric construction
The text describes a circle whose bulge is tangent to a straight line, producing two acute angles. Visualize or draw this construction.
3
Understand the counterexample
The refutation argues that a smaller circle creates an even smaller angle. This shows the original angle is not absolutely smallest and is divisible.
4
Reflect on the existence discussion
Consider the argument about the existence of a sphere and a plane. The refutation affirms their existence, countering the mutakallimūn's possible denial.
This part addresses potential objections about the reality of the geometric entities.

الوجه الثالث : برهن إقليدس في الشكل الخامس عشر من المقالة الثالثة من الأصول على وجود زاوية هي أصغر الزوايا وهي ما تحصل من مماسة خط مستقيم لمحيط دائرة فهي لا تنقسم إذ لو انقسمت لم تكن أصغر الزوايا ولا تتصور الزاوية التي لا تنقسم إلا بإثبات الجزء لأن تلك الزاوية إن كانت جوهراً كانت جزءاً، وإن كانت عرضاً فلا بد لها من محل هو جوهر غير منقسم، والجواب : أن المبرهن في كتابه هو أن الزاوية الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة والخط المماس لها أصغر من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد. قوله : (لا أنها أصغر إلخ) فهي قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ويحصل بالقسمة زاوية بين محيط الدائرة والخط المستقيم أصغر منها . قوله : ( لا مكان إلخ) في الشفاء : لا يدري هل يمكن أنه يوجد كرة على السطح بهذه الصفة في الوجود أو هو في التوهم فقط على نحو ما عليه التعليمات فلا يدري أنه إن كان في الوجود هل يصح مدحرجة أو لا عليه انتهى ولا خفاء في أن منع إمكان وجود الكرة والسطح مكابرة لأن الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط الكرة بل واقعة لأن الأفلاك عندهم كرات حقيقية كذا وجود السطح المستوي لأنه لا شك في وجود السطح فإن كان مستوياً فهو المطلوب وإن كان ذوات زوايا فلا بد من الانتهاء إليه لامتناع اشتماله على السطوح وزوايا غير متناهية وقد مر ذلك في بحث الخلاء . قوله : (هو أنه الزاوية الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة الحدبة بالنقاط الثلاث، وذكر في الصحاح أن الحدب ما ارتفع من الأرض والحدبة التي في الظهر يعني أنا نفرض دائرة يماس حدبتها خطاً مستقيماً بنقطة في وسط هذا الخط فيحدث هناك زاويتان حادتان. ولا شك أن كل واحدة منهما تكون أصغر من كل حادة مستقيمة الضلعين إذا فرض تساويهما في الضلعين والوتر جميعاً، وقوله : لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد إذ لا شك أن الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة الحادة الحادثة الخط المستقيم أصغر من مع ذلك الخط من حدبة الدائرة الصغرى مع المستقيم أيضاً فإن أحد ضلعي الحادة الأولى يكون بين ضلعي الحادة الثانية، فيكون وتر الثانية أطول من وتر الأولى كما يشهد به التخيل الصحيح. الكبرى قوله : (لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد) كما يظهر من أطراف المتممات وأنها أيضاً متفاوتة .
The third proof: Euclid demonstrated in the fifteenth figure of the third article of the Elements the existence of an angle that is the smallest of angles, which is obtained from the tangency of a straight line to the circumference of a circle. So it is indivisible, for if it were divisible it would not be the smallest of angles. And the angle that is indivisible is not conceivable except by affirming the part, because if that angle is a substance, then it is a part, and if it is an accident, then it must have a substrate that is an indivisible substance. The answer: What is demonstrated in his book is that the acute angle produced from the bulge of the circle and the tangent line is smaller than every acute angle with two straight lines, not that it is smaller than all acute angles. His statement: '(Not that it is smaller, etc.)'—that is, it is divisible ad infinitum, and by division an angle between the circumference of the circle and the straight line smaller than it is obtained. His statement: '(No possibility, etc.)' in al-Shifā': It is not known whether a sphere can exist on a surface with this description in existence, or it is only in imagination according to what is in al-Tanbīhāt, so it is not known whether if it existed in existence, it would be correct for it to roll or not, end. And it is not hidden that denying the possibility of the existence of a sphere and a surface is obstinacy, because the natural shape of a simple body is a sphere—rather it is actual, because the celestial spheres according to them are real spheres—and similarly the existence of a plane surface, because there is no doubt in the existence of a surface. If it is plane, then it is what is sought; and if it has angles, then one must eventually end at a plane, because it is impossible for it to contain infinite surfaces and angles, and this has already passed in the discussion of void. His statement: '(It is that the acute angle produced from the bulge of the circle—al-ḥadaba with three dots, and in al-Ṣiḥāḥ it is mentioned that al-ḥadab is what rises from the ground, and al-ḥadaba is the hump on the back)—means we suppose a circle whose bulge is tangent to a straight line at a point in the middle of this line, and there arise two acute angles. There is no doubt that each of them is smaller than every acute straight-sided angle if we suppose them equal in both sides and the chord. And his statement: 'Not that it is smaller than all acute angles'—because there is no doubt that the acute angle produced from the bulge of the circle is smaller with that line than the acute angle produced from the bulge of a smaller circle with the same straight line, because one of the two sides of the first angle lies between the two sides of the second angle, so the chord of the second is longer than the chord of the first, as correct imagination testifies. al-kubrā (?). His statement: '(Not that it is smaller than all acute angles)' as appears from the extremes of the completions, and that they are also varying.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof advanced by the mutakallimūn to establish the existence of the indivisible part (juz' lā yatajazza'), followed by its refutation. The proof is based on Euclid's demonstration in the Elements that there exists an angle which is the smallest of all angles—specifically, the angle formed by the tangency of a straight line to a circle. The mutakallimūn argue that since this angle is the smallest, it must be indivisible; if divided, it would not be the smallest. Moreover, they claim that such an indivisible angle can only be conceived if we admit the existence of an indivisible part (al-juz'), because if the angle is a substance it is itself a part, and if an accident it requires an indivisible substrate. The refutation, however, points out that Euclid's demonstration only proves the angle to be smaller than every acute angle with two straight sides, not smaller than all acute angles generally. Hence it does not entail indivisibility; indeed, it is divisible into even smaller angles. The commentator elaborates by explaining that the angle in question is produced from the bulge (ḥadaba) of the circle and the tangent line, and that it is only relatively smallest. He also discusses the possibility of the existence of a sphere and a plane, citing al-Shifā' and affirming that such existence is actual, not merely imaginary. The commentary further clarifies the geometric construction: a circle with its bulge tangent to a straight line at a midpoint yields two acute angles, each smaller than any straight-sided acute angle of equal sides and chord. The refutation emphasizes that the angle is not absolutely smallest, as demonstrated by considering a smaller circle with the same tangent, which produces an even smaller angle. Thus the proof fails to establish the indivisible part.

Key Terms
حدبة
مماسة
إقليدس
الشفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Euclid's smallest angle is absolutely smallest and thus indivisible, proving the existence of an indivisible part.
Correction: Euclid's angle is only smaller than straight-line acute angles, not all acute angles; smaller angles can be constructed, so it does not prove indivisibility or the indivisible part.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is Euclid's smallest angle not a valid proof for the existence of an indivisible part?
A) Because it is actually divisible in principle, as shown by constructing smaller angles.
B) Because it is only smaller than straight-line acute angles, not all acute angles.
C) Because the angle is a substance, not an accident.
D) Because Euclid's proof is limited to straight lines only.

Key Takeaway
Euclid's smallest angle is only relatively smallest among straight-line angles, and since smaller angles can be constructed, it does not prove indivisibility or the existence of an indivisible part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a proof as final and absolute, only to later realize it was conditional? This lesson shows how a geometric theorem was misapplied in theology. Where in your own understanding of faith might you be clinging to a 'proof' that is actually limited? Will you examine it honestly?
?
The refutation hinges on precise definitions—'not that it is smaller than all acute angles' but only a specific kind. Are there doctrines you hold that you have not defined precisely? Can you afford to leave them vague when eternal truth is at stake?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with a belief built on a misunderstanding of a source—like the mutakallimūn did with Euclid. This lesson calls you to scrutinize the foundations of your knowledge. Will you take the time to trace your convictions back to their original sources, or will you rely on summaries?

Ibn Sina's Refutation of Atomic Composition
Ibn Sina argues that the sphere touches only one point at a time, with intermediate lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that contact between a sphere and a plane occurs only at one point at a time, necessitating divisible motion and ruling out the composition of surfaces from indivisible points.

Core Idea
Indivisible points cannot be adjacent without complete coincidence.

Ibn Sina responds to the mutakallimun's proof by analyzing the nature of contact. He argues that the sphere touches the plane at only one point at a time, and to touch another point requires motion through divisible time and space. This leads to a crucial insight: two indivisible points cannot be connected without completely overlapping, so surfaces cannot consist of successive points. This undermines the atomic theory of the mutakallimun.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the English translation. Notice how Ibn Sina builds his reasoning.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Understand the condition for touching another point
Ibn Sina states that the sphere touches the plane at one point, and to touch another point requires divisible motion in divisible time. This establishes that motion, not simultaneous contact, is needed.
This is the first crucial claim.
3
Grasp the impossibility of adjacency without coincidence
He then argues that two indivisible points cannot be adjacent or connected; they would have to coincide entirely because any connection between indivisibles implies complete overlap.
This is the key logical step.
4
Conclude the necessity of intermediate lines
Therefore, there must be a line between any two points of contact. Hence, the sphere's circumference and the plane are continuous, not composed of discrete atoms.
This refutes the mutakallimun's atomic composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب ابن سينا عن ذلك : بأن الكرة إذا ماست السطح على نقطة فإنها لا تماسه على نقطة أخرى إلا بحركة منقسمة في زمان منقسم ثم إن النقطة الأخرى ليست مجاورة للأولى متصلة بها، وإلا كانت منطبقة عليها إذ لا يمكن أن يتصور اتصال بين أمرين غير منقسيمن إلا بطريق الانطباق بينهما بكليتهما فلا بد أن يكون بين النقطتين خط، وكذا الحال في سائر النقط التي يقع بها التماس بينها فلا يكون محيط الكرة ولا السطح المستوي مركباً من نقط متتالية
And Ibn Sina answered that: the sphere, when it touches the plane at a point, does not touch it at another point except by a divisible motion in a divisible time. Then the other point is not adjacent to the first connected to it; otherwise they would coincide, because it is impossible to conceive connection between two indivisible things except by their complete coincidence. So there must be a line between the two points. The same holds for all the points by which contact occurs between them. Thus neither the circumference of the sphere nor the plane is composed of successive points.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage records Ibn Sina's refutation of the mutakallimun's proof. He argues that the sphere touches the plane at only one point at a time, and to contact another point requires motion through divisible time and space. The two points are not contiguous; if they were, they would coincide entirely, because indivisible things cannot be connected without overlapping. Therefore there must be a line between them, and consequently the surfaces are not composed of successive points (atoms). This undermines the mutakallimun's conclusion that the parts are indivisible.

Key Terms
مماسة
نقطة
حركة منقسمة
زمان منقسم
اتصال
انطباق
متتالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina denies that contact occurs at all.
Correction: Ibn Sina does not deny contact; he denies that it occurs at all points simultaneously without motion. He affirms contact at one point at a time.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why cannot two indivisible points be adjacent without coinciding?
A) Because they are too small
B) Because connection between two indivisible things is impossible except by complete overlap
C) Because there is no line between them
D) Because motion is divisible

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument shows that indivisible points cannot be adjacent without overlapping, disproving the mutakallimun's atomic composition of surfaces.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina argues that two distinct indivisible points cannot be adjacent without coinciding entirely. Think about your own beliefs: have you ever assumed that something simple can be added to another simple thing without changing its nature? How might this error affect your understanding of more complex issues like free will or the nature of the soul?
?
The argument uses divisible motion and time to prove continuity. When you reflect on your own experience of motion through time, do you feel it as continuous or as a series of discrete moments? Ibn Sina's argument challenges the atomistic view – can you identify an area in your life where you treat things as discrete 'atoms' when they might actually be continuous?
?
The mutakallimun used atoms to defend divine omnipotence. Ibn Sina refutes them using logic. Does this refutation weaken reliance on God or strengthen it? Ask yourself: Are you trusting logical arguments more than your direct experience of God's power, or are you using logic to better understand His creation? Commit to examining one area where you might be holding onto a flawed assumption about reality.

Objection and Rebuttal to Ibn Sina's Answer about Contact
Objection and rebuttal regarding contact during motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends Ibn Sina's answer against the objection of infinite regress by invoking the compound nature of rolling motion.

Core Idea
Rolling motion solves the contact paradox through its compound nature of rectilinear and circular motion.

The objection argues that if contact only occurs after motion, then during motion there must be some contact, leading to either stasis or infinite regress. The commentator responds by denying the implication, using the fact that rolling motion allows the sphere to remain in contact with the same point while rotating. This lesson walks through the objection and the rebuttal, revealing a sophisticated understanding of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's argument: if contact on the next point occurs after motion, then during motion contact must be on some point. If it's on the first point, the sphere is stationary while moving; if on an intermediate point, the reasoning repeats, leading to infinite points between two points.
Note the two branches of the objection.
2
Examine the commentator's initial rebuttal
The commentator denies the implication by stating that contact remains on the first point during motion due to the sphere's rotation. The sphere's points change but the point of contact on the plane stays the same.
3
Understand the compound motion explanation
The commentator clarifies that rolling is a combination of rectilinear and circular motion. The circular motion allows persistence of contact on one point of the plane, while rectilinear motion moves the sphere forward.
This is the key insight.
4
Consider the remaining objection
A further objection arises: if there must be a line between two points, then motion on that line either involves contact (leading to intermediate points) or not. The commentator leaves this as a puzzle.
The commentator says 'tammal' (reflect), indicating an open question.
5
Reflect on the lesson
This case shows the subtlety in analyzing motion and contact, and how kalam scholars used physical reasoning to defend philosophical positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : فعلى ما ذكرت لا تحصل المماسة على النقطة الأخرى إلا بعد الحركة ففي حال الحركة لا بد من المماسة فإن كانت المماسة على النقطة الأولى كانت الكرة ساكنة حال كونها متحركة وإن كانت على نقطة متوسطة بينهما لزم خلاف المقدر على أنا ننقل الكلام إلى تلك المتوسطة فوجب إذن أن لا يكون بين نقطتي التماس واسطة فيلزم تتالي النقط لأنا نقول : المماسة على النقطة الأولى، وإن كانت قوله : (فعلى ما ذكرت لا تحصل إلخ ) الصواب من أنه يحصل المماسة لأنه المذكور وليس بمترتب عليه . سابقاً قوله : ( كانت الكرة ساكنة لعدم التغير من الحالة الأولى حال كونها متحركة لأن المفروض أن حال الحركة خلاف المقدر لأن المقدر أن المماسة على النقطة الثانية . قوله : ( ننقل الكلام إلخ) لأنها أيضاً بعد الحركة ففي حال الحركة تكون المماسة على نقطة أخرى يتوسط بين الأولى والمتوسط الأولى وهلم جراً حتى يلزم وجود مماسات ونقاط غير متناهية مع كونها محصورة بين حاصرين بل نقول : جميع هذه المماسات الغير المتناهية حاصلة بعد الحركة ففي حال الحركة لا بد من مماسة أخرى فلم يكن الجميع جميعاً . قوله: (المماسة على النقطة الأولى إلخ) منع للملازمة المستفادة من قوله : فإن كانت المماسة على النقطة الأولى كانت الكرة ساكنة حال كونها متحركة يعني لا نسلم لزوم كونها ساكنة حال كونها متحركة لأن مماسة الكرة على النقطة المعينة من السطح الحادثة لكونها غير منقسم باقية في زمان حركة الدحرجة إلى أن تحصل المماسة على النقطة المعينة الأخرى من السطح لأن الكرة متحركة على نفسها فيتبدل نقاطها مع بقاء المماسة بالنقطة الأولى من السطح وإذا وصل إلى النقطة الثانية من سطح حصل مماسة أخرى باقية مع حركة الكرة على نفسها إلى أن يحصل النقطة الثالثة من السطح وهكذا وفيه بحث إما أولاً : فلان الدحرجة حركة مركبة من قوله : ( لأنا نقول المماسة إلخ ) هذا اختيار للشق الأول ومنع الملازمة قوله : كانت الكرة أن ساكنة حال كونها متحركة وقوله : لكنها باقية في زمان حركة الدحرجة ولعل السر في ذلك هو حركة الكرة المذكورة على السطح المذكور كانت مركبة من الحركة المستقيمة والحركة المستديرة معاً فباعتبار الحركة المستديرة يتصور أن تبقى المسامتة على نقطة واحدة من السطح زماناً وباعتبار الحركة المستقيمة يتصور أن تزول تلك المسامتة بحيث لا تبقى هناك، هذا ولكن بقي أن يقال : إنك قد أقررت أنه لا بد أن يكون بين النقطتين خط فحركة الكرة على هذا الخط إما أن يكون لا بالمماسة وهو باطل لأنه خلاف المفروض ، وإما أن يكون بالمماسة وهذه المماسة يتصور أن تكون على النقطة الأولى أو على النقطة الثانية إذ المفروض هو أن يكون الحركة على الخط فيما بين النقطتين فتعين أن المماسة كانت على نقطة متوسطة بينهما فحينئذ يلزم ما ذكر من أنه خلاف المفروض وأنه ينقل الكلام إلى تلك المتوسطة، فتأمل .
It cannot be said: According to what you mentioned, contact on the other point does not occur except after motion, so in the state of motion contact must occur. If contact is on the first point, then the sphere is stationary while being moving; if it is on a middle point between them, then it contradicts the supposition, and we transfer the discussion to that middle point. So then there must be no interval between the two points of contact, necessitating the succession of points. For we say: Contact is on the first point, and even if... His saying: (According to what you mentioned, it does not occur, etc.) The correct view is that contact occurs because it was mentioned earlier and it is not based on that. Previously. His saying: (The sphere was stationary due to no change from the first state while being moving) because the assumed state of motion contradicts the estimated condition because the estimation was that contact is on the second point. His saying: (We transfer the discussion, etc.) because it also is after motion. So in the state of motion, contact is on another point that lies between the first and the first intermediate, and so on until there would be infinite contacts and points while being bounded between two limits. Rather we say: All these infinite contacts occur after motion, so in the state of motion there must be another contact, so they are not all at once. His saying: (Contact on the first point, etc.) is a denial of the implication derived from his statement: if contact is on the first point, the sphere is stationary while being moving. That is, we do not accept the necessity of its being stationary while being moving, because the contact of the sphere on the specific point of the plane, being indivisible, remains during the time of rolling until contact occurs on the other specific point of the plane, because the sphere moves around itself, so its points change while the contact with the first point of the plane remains. And when it reaches the second point of the plane, another contact occurs that remains with the sphere's motion around itself until the third point of the plane is reached, and so on. But there is a discussion: First, because rolling is a compounded motion of... His saying: (For we say, contact etc.) This is a choice of the first alternative and a denial of the implication. His saying: (The sphere was stationary while being moving) and his saying: (But it remains during the time of rolling). Perhaps the secret in that is that the motion of the mentioned sphere on the mentioned plane was compounded of rectilinear motion and circular motion together. So by virtue of circular motion, it is conceivable that the alignment remains on one point of the plane for a time, and by virtue of rectilinear motion, it is conceivable that that alignment disappears such that it does not remain there. However, it remains to be said: You have acknowledged that there must be a line between the two points. So the motion of the sphere on this line is either without contact, which is false because it contradicts the supposition, or with contact, and this contact can be conceived to be on the first point or on the second point since the supposition is that the motion is on the line between the two points. Then it is determined that the contact was on a middle point between them. Then what was mentioned follows: that it contradicts the supposition and that the discussion is transferred to that middle point. So reflect.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if the sphere only contacts a new point after motion, then during motion it must contact some point. If it contacts the first point while moving, that would mean it is stationary; if it contacts an intermediate point, the reasoning would restart, leading to an infinite regress. The commentator (the author of the sharh) responds by denying the implication: the sphere can remain in contact with the first point during motion due to its rotation (compound motion of rolling). He then discusses a further objection: if there must be a line between points, then the motion on that line either involves contact (which would again lead to an intermediate point) or not. The commentator leaves this with 'tammal' (reflect), indicating a remaining puzzle.

دحرجة
حركة مستقيمة
حركة مستديرة
نقطة
تتالي
غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for a sphere to be in motion, its point of contact with the plane must constantly change.
Correction: The commentator shows that due to the compound nature of rolling, the sphere can rotate in place, maintaining contact with the same point of the plane while its own points change.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator argue that the sphere can remain in contact with the first point while moving?
A) By denying motion altogether
B) By saying the sphere is not really moving
C) By invoking the compound motion of rolling, which includes circular motion
D) By asserting that points are divisible

Key Takeaway
The compound nature of rolling motion (rectilinear + circular) resolves the apparent paradox of contact during motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed that contact must change instantly, leading to a contradiction. In what areas of your life do you assume that change must be immediate or all-or-nothing, ignoring the compound nature of progress? How can you embrace gradual transformation in your spiritual journey?
?
The rebuttal shows that the sphere can be both moving and in contact with the same point due to rotation. Have you ever faced a situation where you thought you had to choose between two opposites (e.g., action vs. contemplation) but later found they could coexist? What does that teach you about how you approach seemingly contradictory aspects of your faith?
?
This lesson requires careful reflection on motion and contact. How much time do you spend reflecting deeply on your beliefs rather than accepting surface-level contradictions? Identify one belief you hold that you have not examined thoroughly, and commit to studying it this week using the tools of reasoned analysis.
