The First Alignment Argument: Distinct Directions
Al-Iji presents the first alignment argument from distinct directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the inherent right-left distinction in any spatial entity forces its divisibility, thereby refuting the indivisible part.

Core Idea
Any occupying thing has distinct sides, so it must be divisible.

This lesson presents the first of two alignment arguments against the indivisible part. Al-Iji argues that any entity that occupies space (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) necessarily has distinct right and left sides, and by extension all opposite directions are different. This multiplicity of directions implies that the entity must be divisible, making the indivisible part impossible. We will examine this argument and its implications for substantial lines and surfaces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on distinct directions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key claim: every occupying thing has right ≠ left.
This is a direct argument from the nature of spatial occupation.
2
Understand the implication for divisibility
Reflect: if right and left are different, the entity has parts; therefore it is divisible. Connect this to the impossibility of the indivisible part.
3
Distinguish substantial vs. accidental extensions
Note that Al-Iji exempts accidental points, lines, and surfaces because they do not occupy space, so the argument does not apply to them.
This nuance is important for the overall theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : الجسم البسيط واحد قابل للقسمة أربعة : ( النوع الأول : ما يتعلق بالمحاذاة وذلك وجهان : الأول : كل متحيز بالذات يمينه غير يساره ضرورة ) وكذا سائر جهاته المتقابلة متغايرة فظهر أن المتحيز بالذات يحب أن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات فاستحال وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وكذا وجود الخط والسطح الجوهريين فضلاً عن تركب الجسم منها بخلاف النقطة والخط والسطح العرضيين فإنها ليست بمتحيزة بذواتها حتى يتصور لها جهات مقتضية لانقسامها . الوجه ( الثاني : إنا إذا ركبنا صفحة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ثم قابلنا بها الشمس فإِن الوجه المضيء من تلك الصفحة (أي) الوجه (الذي إلى الشمس غير الوجه ( المظلم أي الذي إلينا وهذا أيضاً (ضروري) فوجب أن تكون تلك الأجزاء منقسمة. وقد أجيب عن هذين الوجهين بأن اللازم منهما تعدد الأطراف ويجوز أن يكون لشيء واحد غير منقسم في ذاته أطراف هي أعراض حالة فيه، ودفع هذا الجواب بأن الطرفين المحاذيين لليمين واليسار مثلاً، إن كانا جوهرين فهما جزءان للذي فرض غير منقسم وإن كانا عرضين فإما أن يكونا حالين في محل واحد بحيث تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، فيلزم أن يكون ما حاذى منه يمينه عين ما حاذى منه يساره وهو بديهي البطلان، وإما أن يكونا حالين قوله : كل متحيز بالذات يمينه غير يساره يعني أن ما حاذى منه لجهة اليمين غير ما حاذى منه لجهة اليسار، والجواب أن هذا حكم وهمي من قياس غير المنقسم على المنقسم فإنه لعدم انقسامه محاذ بنفسه لكل واحد من الجهات الست فله محاذيات متعددة باعتبار تعدد ما يحاذی به من الجهات وهذه المحاذاة نقطة لمركز نقاطة محيطة بالدائرة فإنها محاذية بنفسها لكل واحد منها وتحقيقه أن المحاذاة من الأمور الاعتبارية التي ينتزعها الوهم من الشيء بالقياس إلى الأمور الواقعة منها وضع مخصوص ويكفي لاعتباره تعدد أحد الطرفين ولا يحتاج إلى تعدد كل واحد منهما كالأبوة المتعددة باعتبار تعدد الأبناء من غير تعدد في ذات الأب، نعم لو كانت المحاذاة عرضاً قائماً بالمحل فلا بد للمحاذتين من محلين فيلزم الانقسام وهذا الجواب مطرد في الاستدلال بنوع المحاذاة . قوله : ( وإما أن يكونا إلخ) بقي هاهنا احتمال وهو أن يكونا حالين في محل واحد لكن لا يتحدان في الإشارة كالنقطتين الحالتين في الخط على زعمهم. فالأوجه أن يقال : إن كانا في محل واحد بحيث يتحدان في الإشارة كان ما حاذى يمينه عين ما حاذى يساره وإن لم يتحدا في الإشارة الحسية يلزم انقسام المحل ولو وهماً يتبع الإشارة إلى الحالين. عن بعضها فقط بحيث يحتجب الإقناع بوجه الإقناع وبالجملة الأدلة المذكورة في النوعين لإثبات الجزء مردود لا يفيد الظن والقدر المشترك إنما يفيده إذا أفاد كل منهما الظن وقد عرفت الأمر.
First observatory - Fifth goal: The simple body is one divisible. Four: (First type: What relates to alignment, and it has two aspects: First: Every inherently occupying space thing: its right side is not its left side necessarily.) And similarly its other opposite directions are different. So it becomes clear that the inherently occupying space thing must be divisible in all directions, so the existence of the indivisible part is impossible, and likewise the existence of the substantial line and surface, let alone the composition of the body from them, unlike the accidental point, line, and surface, for they are not inherently occupying space such that directions requiring their divisibility could be conceived for them. Second aspect: If we compose a page from indivisible parts and then face it toward the sun, then the illuminated face of that page (i.e., the face facing the sun) is other than the dark face (i.e., the one facing us), and this is also necessary. So those parts must be divisible. And it has been answered concerning these two aspects that what is necessary from them is multiplicity of ends, and it is possible for a single indivisible thing to have ends that are accidents inhering in it. And this answer is refuted by saying: the two ends adjacent to the right and left, for example, if they are substances, then they are two parts of that which is assumed indivisible; if they are accidents, then either they inhere in a single locus such that the indication to one of them is the same as the indication to the other -- then it follows that what faces its right is the same as what faces its left, and this is evidently false -- or they are inhering [in two distinct loci, so division follows]. His saying: 'Every inherently occupying space thing: its right side is not its left side' means that what faces it from the direction of the right is not what faces it from the direction of the left. The answer is that this is an imaginary judgment from analogizing the indivisible to the divisible. For due to its indivisibility, it faces by itself each of the six directions, so it has multiple facing things by consideration of the multiplicity of what it faces from directions. This facing is like a point facing a circle's circumference points; it faces each by itself. Its verification is that facing is among relational matters that the imagination extracts from a thing in relation to matters occurring in a specific position. It suffices for its consideration the multiplicity of one of the two sides, and it does not require multiplicity of each one of them, like multiple fatherhoods due to multiple children without multiplicity in the father's essence. Yes, if facing were an accident subsisting in a locus, then two facings would require two loci, so division would follow. This answer is consistent in reasoning by the type of facing. His saying: (And if they are... etc.) There remains here a possibility: that they are inhering in a single locus but not unified in indication, like two points inhering in a line according to their claim. So the best is to say: If they are in a single locus such that they are unified in indication, then what faces its right is the same as what faces its left; if they are not unified in sensible indication, the division of the locus follows, even if imaginatively, following the indication of the two inhering things. ... from some of them only such that the persuasion is hidden by the face of persuasion, and in sum, the proofs mentioned in the two types for establishing the part are rejected, not yielding probability; and the common ground only yields it if each of them yields probability, and you have known the matter.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first type of arguments against the indivisible part based on alignment (muhadhat). It begins by stating the first aspect: any entity that occupies space (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) necessarily has distinct right and left sides, and by extension distinct opposite directions; therefore it must be divisible. Consequently, the indivisible part cannot exist, nor can a substantial line or surface, though accidental points, lines, and surfaces are exempt because they do not occupy space. The second aspect uses a thought experiment: a page composed of indivisible parts facing the sun would have illuminated and dark sides, so the parts must be divisible. An objection is then raised: the argument only proves multiplicity of ends, not division, because ends could be accidents inhering in a single substance. The refutation (daf') analyses the ends: if they are substances, they are parts of the indivisible thing; if accidents, they either inhere in one locus (leading to identity of right and left, which is absurd) or in distinct loci (implying division). The commentator ('qala') then clarifies that the initial claim about right and left is an imaginary judgment from analogizing the indivisible to the divisible. He explains that an indivisible point can face multiple directions relationally, like a point facing all points of a circle. Facing is a relational matter (amr i'tibari) that requires only multiplicity on one side, like fatherhood. However, if facing were a real accident, division would be necessary. A further possibility is considered: two accidents in one locus but with distinct indications, like two points on a line. If they share indication, left and right coincide; if distinct, the locus divides. The passage concludes that these proofs are not probative (la yufid al-zann) unless each individually yields probability.

Key Terms
متحيز بالذات
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
جهات متقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that occupying space does not require distinct sides because a point has no sides.
Correction: Al-Iji's point is that anything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) necessarily has extension, hence distinct directions. Accidental points do not occupy space, so they are not counterexamples.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the first alignment argument?
A) The indivisible part has no sides, so it cannot be divided.
B) Any occupying thing must be divisible, so the indivisible part cannot exist.
C) Accidental points are also divisible.
D) Only the right and left sides differ, not other directions.

Key Takeaway
The first alignment argument shows that any spatial entity must have distinct directions, hence divisibility, making the indivisible part impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every spatial entity must be divisible into parts, what does that reveal about the contingency of your own body? How does this truth change the way you view your physical existence and your dependence on Allah every moment?
?
The argument shows that nothing in the created world can be truly indivisible or self-sufficient. Only Allah is One without parts. Does your heart truly affirm this absolute oneness, or do you still unconsciously treat some created things as independent sources of power or comfort? What would it mean for you to live out tawhid practically today?

The Second Alignment Argument: The Sunlit Page
Al-Iji presents the second alignment argument using a sunlit page.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second alignment argument that a page of indivisible parts facing the sun must have distinct illuminated and dark sides, implying divisibility.

Core Idea
A page of indivisible atoms facing the sun has two distinct sides, so its parts must be divisible.

Al-Iji presents a second argument from alignment: if we compose a page from indivisible parts (atoms) and hold it facing the sun, the side facing the sun is illuminated, while the other side is dark. Since these two sides are distinct, the page must have parts that are divisible. This argument reinforces the conclusion that the indivisible part cannot exist. We will examine this thought experiment and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Imagine the scenario: a page made of indivisible parts turned toward the sun.
2
Identify the two distinct faces
Note that the illuminated face and the dark face are different. This difference forces the page to have spatially distinct parts.
3
Connect to divisibility
If the parts were truly indivisible, they could not have two distinct sides facing different directions. Therefore they must be divisible.
This parallels the earlier right/left argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the entire page could be a single indivisible entity that has two sides as accidents.
Correction: If the page were a single indivisible atom, it could not have distinct faces because its entire 'face' would be the same side. The fact that we can point to different faces shows spatial parts.

Checkpoint
What is the key observation in the second alignment argument?
A) The sun's rays heat the page.
B) The illuminated face and dark face are identical.
C) The illuminated face and dark face are different.
D) The page is made of one indivisible part.

Key Takeaway
The sunlit page argument shows that a collection of indivisible atoms would still have distinct faces, forcing the atoms themselves to be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you treat any of your deepest held beliefs as 'indivisible atoms'—so fundamental that you never examine them for internal contradictions? What might you discover if you held them up to the light today?
?
The sunlit page reveals that even a simple object has hidden complexity. What area of your spiritual life—prayer, charity, patience—have you oversimplified into a single 'indivisible' act? Will you commit to exploring its inner dimensions this week?
?
If a collection of atoms must be divisible to have two faces, then your most consistent daily habits must also have hidden aspects. Which of your public actions hides a private face you have ignored? Will you face that darkness today?

Objection, Refutation, and Final Assessment
Al-Iji presents objection, refutation, and evaluation of alignment arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to alignment arguments, its refutation, and Al-Iji's final assessment that these arguments are not conclusive.

Core Idea
The arguments only prove multiple ends, not division; but careful analysis forces division anyway.

Having presented two alignment arguments, Al-Iji now considers an objection: these arguments only show that the indivisible part has multiple ends, not that it is divisible, because the ends could be accidents inhering in a single substance. He then refutes this objection by analyzing whether those ends are substances or accidents, and in the latter case, whether they inhere in one locus or two. The discussion reveals subtleties about the nature of relational properties (amr i'tibari) and concludes that the proofs are not probative unless each individually yields probability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وقد أجيب' to the end. Identify the objection and the two-part refutation.
2
Understand the substance/accident analysis
The refutation considers whether the ends are substances (then they are parts) or accidents. If accidents, it examines location.
This is a standard kalam tool: dichotomous analysis.
3
Grasp the relational nature of facing
Al-Iji explains that 'facing' (muhadhat) is a relational concept (amr i'tibari) that does not require multiplicity in the subject.
This clarifies why the initial claim is an imaginary judgment.
4
Reflect on the final assessment
The passage ends by saying these proofs do not yield probability (zann) unless each individually does. Understand the moderate conclusion.

المظلم أي الذي إلينا وهذا أيضاً (ضروري) فوجب أن تكون تلك الأجزاء منقسمة. وقد أجيب عن هذين الوجهين بأن اللازم منهما تعدد الأطراف ويجوز أن يكون لشيء واحد غير منقسم في ذاته أطراف هي أعراض حالة فيه، ودفع هذا الجواب بأن الطرفين المحاذيين لليمين واليسار مثلاً، إن كانا جوهرين فهما جزءان للذي فرض غير منقسم وإن كانا عرضين فإما أن يكونا حالين في محل واحد بحيث تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، فيلزم أن يكون ما حاذى منه يمينه عين ما حاذى منه يساره وهو بديهي البطلان، وإما أن يكونا حالين قوله : كل متحيز بالذات يمينه غير يساره يعني أن ما حاذى منه لجهة اليمين غير ما حاذى منه لجهة اليسار، والجواب أن هذا حكم وهمي من قياس غير المنقسم على المنقسم فإنه لعدم انقسامه محاذ بنفسه لكل واحد من الجهات الست فله محاذيات متعددة باعتبار تعدد ما يحاذی به من الجهات وهذه المحاذاة نقطة لمركز نقاطة محيطة بالدائرة فإنها محاذية بنفسها لكل واحد منها وتحقيقه أن المحاذاة من الأمور الاعتبارية التي ينتزعها الوهم من الشيء بالقياس إلى الأمور الواقعة منها وضع مخصوص ويكفي لاعتباره تعدد أحد الطرفين ولا يحتاج إلى تعدد كل واحد منهما كالأبوة المتعددة باعتبار تعدد الأبناء من غير تعدد في ذات الأب، نعم لو كانت المحاذاة عرضاً قائماً بالمحل فلا بد للمحاذتين من محلين فيلزم الانقسام وهذا الجواب مطرد في الاستدلال بنوع المحاذاة . قوله : ( وإما أن يكونا إلخ) بقي هاهنا احتمال وهو أن يكونا حالين في محل واحد لكن لا يتحدان في الإشارة كالنقطتين الحالتين في الخط على زعمهم. فالأوجه أن يقال : إن كانا في محل واحد بحيث يتحدان في الإشارة كان ما حاذى يمينه عين ما حاذى يساره وإن لم يتحدا في الإشارة الحسية يلزم انقسام المحل ولو وهماً يتبع الإشارة إلى الحالين. عن بعضها فقط بحيث يحتجب الإقناع بوجه الإقناع وبالجملة الأدلة المذكورة First observatory - Fifth goal: The simple body is one divisible. Four: (First type: What relates to alignment, and it has two aspects: First: Every inherently occupying space thing: its right side is not its left side necessarily.) And similarly its other opposite directions are different. So it becomes clear that the inherently occupying space thing must be divisible in all directions, so the existence of the indivisible part is impossible, and likewise the existence of the substantial line and surface, let alone the composition of the body from them, unlike the accidental point, line, and surface, for they are not inherently occupying space such that directions requiring their divisibility could be conceived for them. Second aspect: If we compose a page from indivisible parts and then face it toward the sun, then the illuminated face of that page (i.e., the face facing the sun) is other than the dark face (i.e., the one facing us), and this is also necessary. So those parts must be divisible. And it has been answered concerning these two aspects that what is necessary from them is multiplicity of ends, and it is possible for a single indivisible thing to have ends that are accidents inhering in it. And this answer is refuted by saying: the two ends adjacent to the right and left, for example, if they are substances, then they are two parts of that which is assumed indivisible; if they are accidents, then either they inhere in a single locus such that the indication to one of them is the same as the indication to the other -- then it follows that what faces its right is the same as what faces its left, and this is evidently false -- or they are inhering [in two distinct loci, so division follows]. His saying: 'Every inherently occupying space thing: its right side is not its left side' means that what faces it from the direction of the right is not what faces it from the direction of the left. The answer is that this is an imaginary judgment from analogizing the indivisible to the divisible. For due to its indivisibility, it faces by itself each of the six directions, so it has multiple facing things by consideration of the multiplicity of what it faces from directions. This facing is like a point facing a circle's circumference points; it faces each by itself. Its verification is that facing is among relational matters that the imagination extracts from a thing in relation to matters occurring in a specific position. It suffices for its consideration the multiplicity of one of the two sides, and it does not require multiplicity of each one of them, like multiple fatherhoods due to multiple children without multiplicity in the father's essence. Yes, if facing were an accident subsisting in a locus, then two facings would require two loci, so division would follow. This answer is consistent in reasoning by the type of facing. His saying: (And if they are... etc.) There remains here a possibility: that they are inhering in a single locus but not unified in indication, like two points inhering in a line according to their claim. So the best is to say: If they are in a single locus such that they are unified in indication, then what faces its right is the same as what faces its left; if they are not unified in sensible indication, the division of the locus follows, even if imaginatively, following the indication of the two inhering things. ... from some of them only such that the persuasion is hidden by the face of persuasion, and in sum, the proofs mentioned in the two types for establishing the part are rejected, not yielding probability; and the common ground only yields it if each of them yields probability, and you have known the matter.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first type of arguments against the indivisible part based on alignment (muhadhat). It begins by stating the first aspect: any entity that occupies space (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) necessarily has distinct right and left sides, and by extension distinct opposite directions; therefore it must be divisible. Consequently, the indivisible part cannot exist, nor can a substantial line or surface, though accidental points, lines, and surfaces are exempt because they do not occupy space. The second aspect uses a thought experiment: a page composed of indivisible parts facing the sun would have illuminated and dark sides, so the parts must be divisible. An objection is then raised: the argument only proves multiplicity of ends, not division, because ends could be accidents inhering in a single substance. The refutation (daf') analyses the ends: if they are substances, they are parts of the indivisible thing; if accidents, they either inhere in one locus (leading to identity of right and left, which is absurd) or in distinct loci (implying division). The commentator ('qala') then clarifies that the initial claim about right and left is an imaginary judgment from analogizing the indivisible to the divisible. He explains that an indivisible point can face multiple directions relationally, like a point facing all points of a circle. Facing is a relational matter (amr i'tibari) that requires only multiplicity on one side, like fatherhood. However, if facing were a real accident, division would be necessary. A further possibility is considered: two accidents in one locus but with distinct indications, like two points on a line. If they share indication, left and right coincide; if distinct, the locus divides. The passage concludes that these proofs are not probative (la yufid al-zann) unless each individually yields probability.

Misconception: Some may think that the alignment arguments are absolutely conclusive.
Correction: Al-Iji shows that the arguments are not probative; they rely on an imaginary analogy and the final assessment is that they do not yield certainty.

Key Takeaway
The alignment arguments, though initially compelling, are refuted by careful analysis of ends and the relational nature of facing, and are ultimately deemed not probative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something appears to have multiple aspects it must be divided? Consider your understanding of Allah's attributes: have you ever falsely analogized the Creator to creation in this way? What would it take to repent from that analogy?
?
The passage warns against 'imaginary judgments' that analogize the indivisible to the divisible. Where in your life have you made a judgment based on a false analogy that led you to a wrong conclusion about God, yourself, or others? Will you commit to seeking the truth from the source rather than your own imagination?
?
The final verdict says these proofs 'do not yield probability.' Are there beliefs you hold that rest on 'not probative' reasoning? Are you willing to examine them honestly, even if it means letting go of a comforting but weak argument?

The Contact Argument and Refutation of Interpenetration
Al-Iji presents the contact argument against indivisible parts and refutes interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contact argument refutes the existence of indivisible parts and why interpenetration cannot salvage the theory.

Core Idea
If a body is composed of indivisible parts, the middle part must have two distinct contact surfaces, contradicting its indivisibility.

Al-Iji now presents one of the most powerful arguments against the atomistic theory: the contact argument. He shows that if indivisible parts are arranged in a body, the part in the middle must simultaneously block the two ends from touching, which forces it to have two different contact surfaces—hence it must be divisible. Then he anticipates the objection of interpenetration and firmly refutes it using both rational intuition and logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a feel for the flow of the argument. Do not analyze yet; just absorb the overall structure.
Notice that the passage starts by finishing a previous discussion before launching into the contact argument.
2
Identify the core argument
Focus on the contact argument: why does the middle part necessarily divide? Look for the premise that in a composition parts must be contiguous and ordered, and the conclusion that the middle part has two distinct contact surfaces.
The key move is that if the middle touched both ends with the same surface, the ends would be in contact directly, contradicting the blocking.
3
Examine the objection and refutation
Read the objection about interpenetration. Then study the refutation: first, it is impossible by intuition; second, even if granted, it destroys the very notion of composition (no increase in volume, no order).
The refutation shows that interpenetration does not save atomism; it undermines it.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the overall result: the contact argument, together with the refutation of interpenetration, demonstrates that the indivisible part cannot be a constituent of physical bodies. Ask yourself: can the atomist respond without invoking interpenetration?
This argument is considered one of the strongest against atomism in classical Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في محلين متمايزين في الإشارة فيلزم الانقسام ولو فرضاً إذ يمكن حينئذ أن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء كما تشهد به البديهة ( النوع الثاني : ما يتعلق بالمماسة وهو أيضاً ) وجهان : الأول : لو تركب الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فليست ) تلك الأجزاء أجزاء ) لا تتجزأ هذا خلف ) لكونه اجتماعا للنقيضين ( بيانه ) أنه إذا تركب الجسم منها فلا بد لها من أن تكون مجتمعة مترتبة متلاصقة وإلا لم يكن هناك تركب حقيقة وحينئذ فلا شك أن الواقع من تلك الأجزاء ( في وسط الترتيب يحجب الطرفين عن التماس فما به يماس الوسط ( أحد الطرفين غير ما به يماس) الطرف (الآخر) إذ لو كانا متحدين لم يكن الوسط حاجباً للطرفين بل كانا متماسين، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ( فينقسم) الجزء الوسط مع كونه غير منقسم ( لا يقال : لا نسلم ذلك أي حجب الوسط للطرفين حتى يلزم انقسامه ( لجواز التداخل بين تلك الأجزاء ) لأنا نقول : بطلانه (ضروري) فإن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن المتحيز بذاته يمتنع أن يداخل مثله بحيث يصير حجمهما معا كحجم واحد منهما ( وإن سلم جواز التداخل جدلاً فيكون حيزهما أي حيز المتداخلين ( واحداً ) ولا يزداد بانضمام أحدهما إلى الآخر مقدار، وكذا إذا انضم إليهما رابع وخامس وغيرهما من الأجزاء (بالغاً ما بلغ فلا يكون ثمة ترتيب ) بين الأجزاء ) ولا وسط ولا طرف ولا يحصل من تأليفها حجم زائد على حجم كل واحد منها ) وذلك ) كله ( خلاف المفروض ) لأنا فرضنا تركب الجسم الذي هو حجم ممتد في الجهات الثلاث تلك الأجزاء فلا بد أن من يكون بينها ترتيب، وأن يكون هناك وسط وطرف ومع هذا الذي ذكرناه من لزوم خلاف المفروض على تقدير التداخل نقول : ( فالمداخلة ) بين جزئين إنما تكون ( بعد المماسة ) بينهما فلا شك أن الملاقي) من أحد الجزأين ( عند المماسة غير قوله : ( وكذا إذا انضم إليهما رابع وخامس فيه بحث ظاهر لم يجوز التداخل بين اثنين ثلاثة ولا يجوز بين أربعة أو خمسة؟ ولعل المقصود من إيراد هذا الكلام هو التنبيه دون الاستدلال فالمنع هاهنا لا يجدي كثير نفع .
in two distinct loci in indication, so division follows, even if hypothetically, because then it can be assumed that in it there is something other than something, as intuition bears witness. (Second type: What relates to contact, and it also has two aspects: First: If the body is composed of indivisible parts, then those parts are not indivisible, this is a contradiction because it is a conjunction of opposites. Explanation: When the body is composed of them, they must be gathered, ordered, and contiguous; otherwise there would be no real composition. Then there is no doubt that the one among those parts located in the middle of the order blocks the two ends from contact. So that by which the middle part touches one end is other than that by which it touches the other end, because if they were identical, the middle would not block the two ends, rather they would be in contact. And when this is the case, the middle part divides, despite being indivisible. It cannot be said: We do not accept this, i.e., the blocking of the middle for the two ends such that its division follows, due to the permissibility of interpenetration among those parts. For we say: Its falsity is necessary, because the intuition of the intellect bears witness that an inherently occupying space thing is impossible to interpenetrate another like it such that their combined volume becomes like the volume of one of them. And even if interpenetration is granted as dialectical, then the space of the two, i.e., the space of the interpenetrating things, would be one and does not increase by the joining of one to the other in magnitude. Similarly, if a fourth, fifth, and other parts join them, reaching however many, there would be no order among the parts, nor middle, nor end, and no volume greater than the volume of each one results from their composition. All of that contradicts the assumption, because we assumed that the body, which is an extended volume in three dimensions, is composed of those parts. So there must be order among them, and there must be a middle and an end. And with this that we have mentioned of the necessity of contradicting the assumption on the estimation of interpenetration, we say: Interpenetration between two parts occurs only after contact between them. There is no doubt that the meeting part from one of the two parts at the moment of contact is other than [the meeting part from the other?] His saying: 'And likewise if a fourth and fifth join them': there is an apparent investigation: why is interpenetration not permissible between two but permissible between four or five? Perhaps the purpose of mentioning this speech is attention without argumentation; so the prevention here does not provide much benefit.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect of the second type of argument against the indivisible part, based on contact (mumassa). It begins by completing a previous discussion: if accidents are in distinct loci, division follows necessarily even hypothetically. Then it introduces the contact argument: if a body is composed of indivisible parts, those parts must be arranged contiguously; otherwise there is no real composition. The part in the middle necessarily blocks the two ends from touching each other. Consequently, the surface by which the middle touches one end must be different from that by which it touches the other end; otherwise the ends would touch directly. Hence the middle part must be divisible into at least two distinct surfaces, contradicting its assumed indivisibility. An objection is raised: perhaps interpenetration (tadakhul) among the parts allows them to overlap, so the middle does not truly block. The refutation is twofold. First, interpenetration is impossible (bāṭil) by the intuition of the intellect: an occupying body cannot occupy the same space as another, as their volumes would not combine. Second, even if interpenetration is hypothetically granted, the space of the interpenetrating parts would remain one, no increase in volume occurs, and no order, middle, or end results — contradicting the assumed spatial extension and composition of the body. Furthermore, interpenetration presupposes prior contact (mumassa) between parts, which itself would require division at the contact surfaces. The commentator notes that the mention of adding many parts is illustrative rather than argumentative, as the impossibility of interpenetration between two suffices.

تداخل

وسط

حجم
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think interpenetration is a viable escape from the contact argument.
Correction: The passage shows interpenetration is impossible by rational intuition, and even if hypothetically granted, it would nullify the very conditions of composition (order, volume increase) required by the original assumption.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the contact argument against the indivisible part?
A) Indivisible parts cannot be arranged in a body.
B) The middle part in a composition must have distinct contact surfaces, forcing its division.
C) Interpenetration allows parts to overlap, so no contact occurs.
D) The ends of a body do not need to be separated by a middle part.

Key Takeaway
The contact argument shows that the indivisible part cannot exist because it would require division at the contact surfaces; interpenetration is impossible and would not preserve the body's extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument relies on the 'intuition of the intellect' (badīha) to refute interpenetration. When you face a claim that contradicts your clear intuition (like that two bodies cannot occupy one space), do you trust your God-given reason, or do you allow sophistry to shake your certainty? In what areas of your faith have you let doubt override clear intuition?
?
The atomists assumed indivisible parts without thinking through the consequences. Can you identify a belief you hold that, when you really follow its logical implications, leads to a contradiction? What would it cost you to abandon that belief for the truth?
?
If interpenetration were possible, the entire physical order would collapse — no size, no distance, no separate things. This world of measure and distinction is a sign of its Creator's wisdom and power. When you see the precise order of your own body or the universe, do you pause in gratitude to the One who upholds it moment by moment? How can you remind yourself of this dependence today?

The Bucket and Hook: Proving the Leap
Al-Iji presents and explains the fifth image (bucket and hook) to argue for the leap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the bucket and hook thought experiment demonstrates the coexistence of fast and slow motion, and how this is used to argue against atomism and for the doctrine of the leap.

Core Idea
A bucket and hook moving together at different speeds disproves atomism.

The fifth image in the series challenges atomism by considering two objects moving concurrently at different speeds. Al-Iji presents an-Naẓẓām's argument and then clarifies the mechanics and implications, ultimately supporting the doctrine of the leap (ṭafrah). This lesson unpacks the setup, the motion, and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the concrete scenario of the bucket and hook in the well.
Pay attention to the spatial relationships and distances mentioned.
2
Visualize the setup
Draw or imagine the well: the bucket at the bottom, the hook at the midpoint, both attached to ropes. Understand how pulling the rope causes both to rise.
The hook is tied at the peg, the bucket at the other end of the rope.
3
Identify the speeds
Notice that the bucket traverses the full depth (100 cubits) while the hook traverses half (50 cubits) in the same time. Therefore, the bucket moves faster than the hook.
This disproves an-Naẓẓām's assumption of equal speeds.
4
Understand the conclusion
The coexistence of fast and slow motion without interruption refutes atomism and provides evidence for the leap, as it shows that motion can skip over indivisible parts.
The commentator also notes issues with the straightness of the line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة الخامسة : دلو على رأس حبل مشدود طرفه الآخر بوتد في وسط البئر مع كلاب يجعل في ذلك الحبل عند الوتد ويمد به فالدلو والكلاب يصلان إلى رأس البئر معاً. فالدلو قطع مسافة البئر حين ما قطع الكلاب نصفه من غير وقوف للكلاب ضرورة فقد تلازمت حركة سريعة وبطيئة، وقد توهم النظام تساوي هاتين الحركتين في السرعة فاستدل بذلك على الطفرة. قوله : ( إنما يقع بخط إلخ) وفيه أن الاستقامة الحقيقية محل بحث، والاستقامة الحسية التخيلية ينافي عدم الاستقامة الحقيقية. قوله : ( مع كلاب) ظرف مستقر وقع حالاً من الضمير المستكن في الظرف أعني قوله على رأس حبل الراجع إلى الدلو وكيفية أن يكون الدلو مشدودة بطرف الحبل واقعاً في البئر ويكون الطرف مشدوداً بالوتد الذي في شط البئر ويكون الكلاب متعلقاً بذلك الحبل عند الوتد قائمة ذلك الحبل بالكلاب بأن يمد حبل الكلاب يكون وصول الكلاب والوتد معاً إلى رأس البئر، وحركة الدلو سريعة لأنها قطعت كل مسافة البئر وحركة الكلاب بطيئة لقطعها نصف مسافة . قوله : ( فالدلو قطع مسافة البئر إلخ مثلاً إذا فرضناً بئراً عمقها مئة ذراع وفي منتصفها خشبة شد عليها طرف حبل طوله خمسون ذراعاً وعلى طرفه الآخر دلو ثم شددنا كلاباً معرب قلاب على طرف حبل طوله خمسون أيضاً، وأرسلناه في البئر بحيث وقع الكلاب في الحبل الأول على طرفه المشدود في الخشبة ثم جررناه إلى رأس البئر فيكون ابتداء حركة الكلاب من الوسط والدلو من الأسفل معا وكذا انتهاؤهما إلى رأس البئر وقد قطع الدلو مئة ذراع والكلاب خمسين فقد تلازمت حركة سريعة وبطيئة. كذا في المقاصد
The fifth form: A bucket on a rope whose other end is tied to a peg in the middle of the well, with a hook placed on that rope at the peg, and it is pulled. So the bucket and the hook both reach the top of the well together. So the bucket traversed the distance of the well when the hook traversed half of it without stopping necessarily, so a fast and slow motion coexisted. An-Naẓẓām mistakenly thought these two motions were equal in speed and used that to argue for the leap. His statement: 'It only occurs by a line, etc.' But in that, the true straightness is a matter of investigation, and the sensory imaginary straightness contradicts the absence of true straightness. His statement: 'with a hook' is a circumstantial adverb from the implicit pronoun in the adverb, namely his saying 'on a rope' referring to the bucket. The manner is that the bucket is tied to the end of the rope lying in the well, and the end is tied to the peg on the rim of the well, and the hook is attached to that rope at the peg. The rope is pulled by the hook, so the hook and the peg both reach the top of the well together. The bucket's motion is fast because it traversed the entire distance of the well, while the hook's motion is slow because it traversed half the distance. His statement: 'The bucket traversed the distance of the well, etc.' For example, if we suppose a well with a depth of one hundred cubits, and in its middle a board on which one end of a rope of fifty cubits is tied, and on the other end a bucket. Then we tie a hook (the Arabicized form of قلاب) on the end of another rope also fifty cubits long, and we lower it into the well such that the hook falls on the first rope at its end tied to the board. Then we pull it to the top of the well. So the hook's motion begins from the middle and the bucket's from the bottom together, and both end at the top. The bucket has traversed one hundred cubits and the hook fifty, so a fast and slow motion coexisted. Thus in al-Maqāṣid.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth ‘image’ (ṣūra) in a series of arguments against atomism by demonstrating the necessity of the doctrine of ‘leap’ (ṭafrah). The image involves a bucket and a hook on ropes in a well. The original argument is attributed to an-Naẓẓām, who thought that the simultaneous arrival of the bucket (traversing the full depth) and the hook (traversing half) implied equal speeds, and he used this to argue for the leap. The commentator clarifies the scenario: the bucket is tied to one end of a rope, the other end is tied to a peg at the rim of the well. A hook is attached to the rope at the peg. When the rope is pulled, both bucket and hook reach the top together. The bucket covers the entire depth, while the hook covers half, so their speeds differ, yet they move concurrently. This is used to refute atomism because if matter were composed of indivisible parts, such concurrent motions of different speeds would be impossible or would require the ‘leap’ (skipping over parts). The commentator also explains that the straightness of the line involved is not truly straight; it is only imaginary, which undermines the argument if one insists on true straightness.

Key Terms
طفرة
حركة سريعة
حركة بطيئة
استقامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An-Naẓẓām thought the bucket and hook must have equal speeds.
Correction: The bucket traverses a greater distance in the same time, so its speed is faster; the speeds are not equal.

Checkpoint
What does the simultaneous arrival of the bucket and hook demonstrate?
A) The equality of speeds
B) The coexistence of fast and slow motion
C) The impossibility of motion
D) The existence of indivisible parts

Key Takeaway
The bucket and hook image shows that motions of different speeds can occur together, which refutes atomism and supports the doctrine of the leap.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in a God who creates atoms and motion. But does your daily life reflect a belief that God can bring about sudden changes without gradual steps? When was the last time you truly trusted God for a 'leap' in your circumstances, or do you insist on seeing incremental progress?
?
This passage corrects an-Naẓẓām's assumption that motion must be uniform. In your own life, do you assume that spiritual growth must be uniform—that every day should feel like progress? How does the coexistence of fast and slow 'motions' in God's creation free you from comparing your pace to others?
?
An-Naẓẓām was a great scholar but he made a mistake in assuming two motions were equal. How does this remind you to be humble in your own reasoning about theological matters? Are you willing to accept logical evidence that contradicts your preconceived ideas?

Sixth Image: A Part Moving on a Moving Line
Al-Iji presents the sixth image using a moving line to argue for the divisibility of the part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji uses a geometric thought experiment to argue that an atomistic part must be divisible.

Core Idea
Two motions of different speeds in one part prove its divisibility.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument against atomism using a mathematical thought experiment. Al-Iji imagines a part moving on a moving line and shows that it results in two concurrent motions of different speeds, which would be impossible if the part were truly indivisible. This is one of several images designed to demonstrate the logical consequences of atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note that this is a hypothetical scenario, not a description of physical reality.
The text uses letters to represent points and lines; visualize them as a diagram.
2
Identify the components of the thought experiment
Identify the stationary line (A B J), the moving line (K H), and the part (Z) on K H. Understand their initial positions: K opposite A, H opposite B, Z at K.
The lines are composed of parts, but for atomists these parts are indivisible.
3
Follow the motion
The line K H moves on A B from A to K, so H moves from B to J. Simultaneously, Z moves on K H from K to H. Trace the final alignments: Z becomes opposite J, K becomes opposite B.
Z moves by its own motion (on K H) and the line's motion, totaling two parts.
4
Understand the conclusion
Z moves two parts while K moves only one part. This establishes two concurrent motions of different speeds for Z, implying that the part (Z) must be divisible.
If Z were truly a single atom, it could not have two distinct motions simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزء يتحرك جزءا على خط متحرك جزء آخر في جهة حركة ذلك الجزء. قوله : ( جزء يتحرك إلخ) أصحاب الجزء لا يقولون بوجود الجزء على الانفراد فضلاً الحركة فهذا تخيل محض من قبيل التعليميات. ثم قال: ولنفرض ا ب ج خطاً ساكناً من أجزاء ثلاثة ونفرض أيضاً كـ هـ خطاً مركباً من جزأين كائناً على ا ب بحيث يكون كـ واقعاً بإزاء ا و هـ واقعاً بإزاء ب و نفرض ز جزءاً كائناً على كـ من خط كـهـ بحيث يلزم من حركة هذا الخط حركته هكذا ز فإذا تحرك كـ بحركة خط كـ هـ على خط ا ب ج من ا إلى كـ فقد تحرك هـ بتلك الحركة من ب إلى ج وفرضنا مع ذلك تحرك ز على خط كـ هـ من كـ وكان أي كـ مقابلاً لـا في ابتداء الفرض إلى هـ أي تحرك زمن كـ إلى هـ وهو وإن كان مقابلاً لـ ب إبتداء لكنه الآن مقابل لـ فيكون ز حينئذ مقابلاً لـ ج أيضاً. فقد تحرك ز بمجموع حركته الذاتية والعرضية جزأين حيت تحرك كـ بتحركة واحدة جزءاً واحداً فإن ز، و كـ كانا معاً محاذيين لـا من خط ا ب ج قبل الحركة والآن قد صار ز محاذيال ج، و كـ محاذياً لـ ب فقد ثبت حرکتان متلازمتان سريعة وبطيئة وهو المطلوب وإِن شئت قلت : فحين تحرك ز بمجموع حركته جزءاً واحداً يكون كـ تحرك أقل من جزء وفيه المراد الذي هو انقسام الجزء.
A part moves a part on a moving line, another part in the direction of motion of that part. His statement: 'a part moves, etc.' The proponents of the part do not acknowledge the existence of the part in isolation, let alone motion, so this is pure imagination from the hypothetical categories. Then he said: Let us suppose A B J a stationary line composed of three parts, and also suppose K H a line composed of two parts existing on A B such that K is opposite A and H is opposite B, and we suppose Z a part existing on K from the line K H such that from the motion of this line, its motion is like this Z. So if K moves with the motion of line K H on line A B J from A to K, then H has moved with that motion from B to J, and we supposed along with that the motion of Z on line K H from K, and K was opposite A at the beginning of the supposition, to H i.e. Z moved from K to H, and though H was initially opposite B, it is now opposite (something), then Z at that moment is also opposite J. So Z has moved by the sum of its own motion and accidental motion two parts, while K moved by one motion one part. For Z and K were both aligned with A on line A B J before motion, and now Z has become aligned with J and K aligned with B. So two concurrent motions have been established, one fast and one slow, which is what was desired. And if you wish, you say: When Z moves by the sum of its motion one part, K moves less than one part, and in this is the desired goal which is the division of the part.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth image (ṣūra) in the argument against atomism. It is a mathematical demonstration using a diagram of lines and parts to show that a part moving on a moving line results in two simultaneous motions of different speeds, which implies that the part must be divisible. The commentator first notes that adherents of atomism do not even accept the existence of a part in isolation, so this is a hypothetical argument. Then the proof is given: suppose a stationary line A B J composed of three parts, and a moving line K H (composed of two parts) placed on it. A part Z is placed on K H. When K H moves from A to K, H moves from B to J. Z moves on K H from K to H. By analyzing the alignments, it is shown that Z moves two parts (its own motion and the motion of the line) while K moves only one part, resulting in motions of different speeds. This is used to argue that the part of the moving line must be divisible, contradicting atomism.

حركة ذاتية

طفرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The demonstration proves actual physical divisibility of a part in reality.
Correction: The demonstration is a logical thought experiment; it does not assume actual parts but uses a hypothetical scenario to show a contradiction in atomism.

Checkpoint

A) The equality of speeds
B) The indivisibility of the part
C) The divisibility of the part
D) The existence of void

Key Takeaway
The sixth image uses a moving line thought experiment to show that an atomistic part must be divisible because it can have two simultaneous motions of different speeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, a logical thought experiment forces you to accept that a seemingly simple part is actually divisible. Is there a belief or practice in your own life that you treat as indivisible—something you refuse to examine or change? How can you allow the evidence of your own heart and actions to 'divide' that assumption for your growth?
?
The proof shows that two simultaneous motions can exist in one part, leading to a contradiction if it were truly one. Are there two competing 'motions' in your life—e.g., a desire for comfort and a call to sacrifice—that you try to reconcile by pretending they are one? What would it take to honestly acknowledge the tension and let it reshape you?
?
Al-Iji presents a hypothetical scenario to break down a false certainty. Recall a time when you held a belief so firmly that you dismissed logical challenges. How can you cultivate the humility to entertain such 'thought experiments' about your own convictions, even when they are uncomfortable?

The Geometric Argument: Square and Diagonal
Al-Iji presents the geometric argument against atomism using a square and diagonal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji uses a square and diagonal to refute atomism by showing a contradiction with Euclidean geometry.

Core Idea
Atomism forces the diagonal to equal the side, contradicting geometry.

Al-Iji presents one of the most direct geometric refutations of atomism. By constructing a square from indivisible parts, he demonstrates that under the atomist framework the diagonal must equal the side—an impossibility proven by Euclidean geometry. This lesson walks through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's construction come through before interpreting.
Notice the use of 'أجزاء' (parts) which are the indivisible minima.
2
Understand the square construction
Al-Iji assumes a square where each side consists of four parts. Count the total parts (16) and how the diagonal is supposedly formed.
3
See why the diagonal equals the side
Under atomism, the diagonal composed of parts must also be four parts, because it goes from one corner to the opposite without gaps.
4
Recognize the contradiction
Euclidean geometry proves the diagonal of a square is longer than the side (hypotenuse). The atomist conclusion contradicts this, so atomism is false.
The proof references the figure 'al-Arūs' (the bride).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع : ما يتعلق بالأشكال الهندسية وهو وجوه ستة : الأول : أنا نفرض مربعاً من أربعة خطوط كل خط منها من أربعة أجزاء ونجتهد في ضم الخطوط بعضها إلى بعض غاية الاجتهاد فذلك المربع ستة عشر جزءاً هكذا فيكون كل ضلع من المربع أربعة أجزاء والقطر الواصل بين طرفي ضلعين محيطين بزاوية أيضاً أربعة أجزاء لأنه إنما يحصل من الجزء الأول الخط الأول والثاني من الثاني والثالث من الثالث والرابع من الرابع فالقطر كالضلع في المقدار وأنه محال بشهادة الحس والبراهين الهندسية الدالة على أن وتر الزاوية القائمة أطول من كل واحد من ضلعيها لأن مربعه يساوي مربعيهما، كما بين في الشكل المسمى بالعروس، وأيضاً إذا كان إحدى زوايا المثلث قائمة كانت الباقيتان حادتين والزاوية العظمى بوترها الضلع الأطول. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز في المربع المذكور أن يكون القطر أطول و ذلك بأن يقع بينها خلاء دون أجزاء الضلع لأنا نقول : الخلاء الذي بين كل جزأين من أجزاء القطر إن وسع جزءاً كان القطر مثل مجموع الضلعين لأنه حينئذ سبعة أجزاء هي الأربعة المذكورة والثلاثة الواقعة في الفرج الثلاث بين جميع تلك الأربعة، لأن وقوع الفرجة . قوله : ( ما يتعلق بالأشكال الهندسية إلخ ) ثبوت الأشكال الهندسية موقوف على وجود المقدار المتوقف على اتصال الجسم في نفسه المتوقف على نفي الجزء، فالاستدلال بها على نفي الجزء دور فعند أصحابه لا زاوية ولا وتر ولا قطر ولا دائرة إنما هي تخيلات باطلة ولعدم
The fourth type: What relates to geometric shapes, and it has six aspects. The first: Let us suppose a square from four lines, each line of them from four parts, and we strive to join the lines to each other with utmost effort. So that square is sixteen parts, thus each side of the square is four parts, and the diagonal connecting the ends of two sides enclosing an angle is also four parts, because it is obtained from the first part the first line, the second from the second, the third from the third, the fourth from the fourth. So the diagonal is equal to the side in magnitude, and that is impossible by testimony of sense and geometric proofs which show that the hypotenuse of a right angle is longer than each of its two sides because its square equals their squares, as shown in the figure called 'al-Arūs'. Also, if one of the angles of a triangle is right, the remaining are acute, and the greatest angle is with its hypotenuse the longest side. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible for the mentioned square that the diagonal be longer, and that by there occurring between them a void not present in the side? For we say: The void between every two parts of the diagonal, if it accommodates a part, then the diagonal would be equal to the sum of the two sides, because it would then be seven parts – the four mentioned and the three occurring in the three gaps between all those four, because the occurrence of the gap... His statement: 'What relates to geometric shapes, etc.' The establishment of geometric shapes depends on the existence of magnitude, which depends on the continuity of the body in itself, which depends on the negation of the part. So using them to negate the part is circular. According to its proponents, there is no angle, no hypotenuse, no diagonal, no circle; they are only invalid imaginations, and...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth type of argument against atomism, based on geometric shapes. It begins with the first of six aspects: a square constructed from four lines each composed of four indivisible parts. According to the atomist framework, the side would be four parts, and the diagonal connecting opposite vertices would also be four parts if the diagonal is composed of a sequence of parts. However, Euclidean geometry proves that the diagonal of a square is longer than the side (the hypotenuse of a right triangle is longer than either leg). Therefore, the atomist assumption leads to a contradiction. An objection is raised: perhaps the diagonal is longer because there are voids between its parts. The answer is that if those voids accommodate whole parts, then the diagonal would become a sum of the two sides (seven parts), which is also false. The commentator adds a methodological remark: using geometric shapes to negate the part is circular because the existence of geometric shapes themselves depends on continuous magnitude, which presupposes the negation of atomism. Thus, for atomists, geometric entities like angles, hypotenuses, diagonals, and circles are invalid imaginations.

Key Terms
مربع
قطر
وتر
زاوية قائمة
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The geometric argument shows that atomism is consistent with geometry.
Correction: Actually, the argument shows atomism leads to a contradiction with Euclidean geometry, thus refuting atomism.

Checkpoint
What does the geometric argument demonstrate if the diagonal equals the side?
A) Atomism is true
B) Atomism leads to geometric contradiction
C) Void is necessary
D) The square is impossible

Key Takeaway
The atomist assumption forces the diagonal to equal the side, contradicting Euclidean geometry, so atomism is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear proof that contradicts a belief you hold dearly, do you instinctively resist or submit to the truth? Recall a recent example from your own life: what did your reaction reveal about your sincerity in seeking truth?
?
Al-Iji appeals to both sensory observation and rational proof. Are you willing to accept arguments from both sources when they challenge your worldview? Where in your life are you ignoring one or the other to avoid discomfort?
?
The atomist assumption led to a contradiction with geometric reality. What assumptions in your own life—about yourself, others, or religion—might be similarly contradicted by clear evidence? Are you ready to abandon them if proven false?

Objection and Circularity in the Geometric Argument
Al-Iji responds to an objection about voids and the commentator notes circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomist's attempted reply (voids) and the commentator's critique that the geometric argument is circular.

Core Idea
The atomist objection fails, and using geometry against atomism may be circular.

Al-Iji anticipates an atomist rejoinder: perhaps the diagonal is longer due to voids between parts. He refutes this by showing it leads to an even greater absurdity. The commentator then raises a deeper methodological concern: geometric shapes themselves presuppose continuous magnitude, so the argument may be circular. This lesson examines both the objection and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage for the objection (لا يقال) and the response. Note how al-Iji counters the void hypothesis.
2
Understand the commentator's note
The commentator points out that geometric shapes depend on continuity, which assumes atomism is false. Thus, using geometry to disprove atomism is circular.
3
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider the strength of each move: the objector tries to save atomism with voids, but the defender shows it leads to a worse result. The commentator then questions the whole enterprise.
This is a common pattern in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the commentator, why is the geometric argument against atomism circular?

?

Are there concepts you hold as real that, if pressed, might only be true because you have already assumed their reality? What is one such concept, and will you examine its foundation this week?
?
Al-Iji shows that every attempted escape from a logical contradiction only leads to a deeper one. Think of a time when you tried to avoid a difficult truth by constructing a more complex justification. Did that justification collapse? What truth were you avoiding, and will you face it now?

The Simple Body: Continuous and Infinitely Divisible
Defines the simple body as continuous and divisible to infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a simple body as a continuous substance divisible to infinity, and how this differs from actual composition of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
A simple body is a continuous whole, not a pile of indivisible atoms.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a foundational question: what is a simple body? The author argues that it is neither composed of indivisible atoms nor compounds thereof, but is a single continuous entity that can be divided without end. This sets the stage for understanding matter in the kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the technical terms: جسم بسيط, جزء لا يتجزأ.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify the two main claims: (1) the simple body is not separated into indivisible parts, (2) it is divisible to infinity.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage says that if division had a limit, that would imply existence of an indivisible part at that limit, which it rejects. Understand why the author argues for infinite divisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالبرهان ( أن الجسم البسيط كالماء مثلاً ) لا ينفصل إلى أجزاء لا تتجزأ )، وما في حكمها من الجواهر المنقسمة في جهة واحدة أو في جهتين ( فقط، فقد ثبت أنه متصل واحد في الحقيقة لا مفصل فيه أصلا ، ( كما هو عند الحس وقابل للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ) أي لا تصل قسمته إلى حد تقف عنده كما مر ، وإلا لزم وجود الجزء عند انتهاء القسمة والحاصل أن ذلك الجسم ليس مركباً بالفعل من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، وما في حكمها فيكون متصلاً في نفسه ولا نفسه ولا تنتهي قسمته إليها، فيكون قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية
By proof: the simple body, such as water, is not separated into indivisible parts, nor into what is in their category from the substances divided in one dimension or in two dimensions only. It has been established that it is one continuous whole in reality, without any separation in it, as is evident to sense, and it is divisible to infinity—that is, its division does not reach a limit at which it stops, as passed, otherwise the part would [come into] existence at the end of division. The result is that that body is not actually composed of indivisible parts, nor of what is in their category, so it is continuous in itself and its division does not end at them, so it is capable of infinite divisions.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage sets out the definition of a simple body (al-jism al-basīṭ) as proven by demonstration. It states that a simple body like water does not actually separate into indivisible atoms (ajzāʾ lā tatajazzaʾ) nor into substances that are considered similar to atoms, i.e., divisible only in one or two dimensions. Rather, it is one continuous entity with no actual division, as perceived by the senses. Moreover, it is potentially divisible to infinity: its division never reaches a final atomic part at which it would have to stop. If it did stop, that would imply the existence of an indivisible part at the endpoint of division. Therefore, the body is not actually composed of such parts, but is a continuum whose divisibility is endless.

Key Terms

جزء لا يتجزأ
اتصال

غير نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simple body is actually composed of infinitely many parts.
Correction: The text says it is divisible to infinity potentially, not that it actually has infinite parts.

Checkpoint
What does the text claim about the simple body's composition?
A) It is actually composed of indivisible atoms.
B) It is not actually composed of indivisible parts and is continuous.
C) It is composed of atoms divisible only in one dimension.
D) It is composed of five elements.

Key Takeaway
A simple body is a continuous substance that is potentially divisible without end, not actually composed of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that the simplest physical thing, like water, is not made of ultimate indivisible parts but is a continuous whole held together by nothing other than the will of its Creator. When you look at the objects around you, do you see them as self-sufficient substances, or as signs that depend on Allah's continuous act of existence? How will this knowledge change the way you interact with the material world today?
?
The proof shows that there is no smallest part—division can go on forever. This means the physical world is not ground-level reality; it points beyond itself. In your own life, where do you tend to stop at the surface—treating things as final and self-standing—rather than pushing deeper to see the Hand of Allah? What one step can you take this week to look through the creation to the Creator?
?
If every simple body is actually a unified whole despite appearing as a collection of particles, consider your own sense of self. You may feel composed of many parts: thoughts, desires, habits. But your essence is a unified soul entrusted by Allah. What part of your identity do you cling to as if it were an indivisible atom? Are you willing to surrender that illusion and recognise that your true continuity lies in your connection to Allah, not in any fragmented self-image?

The Three Types of Division in a Body
Enumeration and explanation of three types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct ways a body can be divided according to Islamic theology: physical separation, differentiation by accidental qualities, and mental estimation.

Core Idea
Division can be physical, by accidental differences, or by mental estimation.

The mutakallimūn carefully analyzed the concept of division to understand the nature of bodies. In this passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif, the author enumerates three types of division and provides brief clarifications. Pay close attention to the distinctions he makes, especially between breaking and cutting, and between stable and unstable accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the concise style and the parenthetical clarifications.
The Arabic uses parentheses to add detail.
2
Identify the three main types
The passage lists three types: physical separation (fakk), difference of accidents (ikhtilāf ʿaraḍayn), and mental estimation (wahm wa fard). Find each in the text.
The first type has two subtypes: breaking and cutting.
3
Understand the key distinctions
Focus on the difference between breaking and cutting, and between stable and unstable accidents. The commentary will help clarify.
These distinctions are essential for kalam discussions about bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسمة إما بالفك ( كسراً أو قطعاً، والفرق بينهما أن القطع يحتاج إلى آلة نفاذة فاصلة بالنفوذ دون الكسر وأيضاً للقطع نوع اختصاص بالأجسام اللينة والكسر بالأجسام الصلبة ) وإما باختلاف عرضين قارين ( في محلهما لا بالقياس إلى غيره ) كالسواد والبياض أو غير قارين ( في المحل باعتبار نفسه بل بالإضافة إلى غيره ) كمماستين ومحاذاتين وإما بالوهم والفرض، فهذه الثلاثة وجوه القسمة في الجسم.
Division is either by [physical] separation (fakk): breaking or cutting, and the difference between them is that cutting requires a penetrating tool that separates by penetration, unlike breaking. Also, cutting has a specific association with soft bodies, and breaking with hard bodies. Or [secondly] by the difference of two accidental qualities (ʿaraḍān) that are stable (qārrin) in their locus, not relative to something else, like blackness and whiteness; or two unstable accidental qualities, like two tangential contacts or two facing contacts. Or [thirdly] by estimation (wahm) and mental supposition (fard). These are the three types of division in a body.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage classifies the ways in which a body can be divided. The first type, al-fakk (physical separation), is subdivided into kasr (breaking) and qaṭʿ (cutting). The text explains the distinction: cutting requires a sharp tool that penetrates, whereas breaking does not necessarily require such a tool. Additionally, cutting is typically applied to soft objects, breaking to hard ones. The second type of division is through the difference of two accidental qualities (ikhtilāf ʿaraḍayn). These accidents can be either stable (qārrin), like blackness and whiteness that coexist in different loci, or unstable (ghayr qārrin), like two instances of contact or facing. The third type is division by estimation (wahm) and supposition (fard), which is a mental operation. The passage concludes by stating these are the three modes of division.

Key Terms
قسمة
فك
كسر
قطع
عرض
وهم
فرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that division by difference of accidents implies actual physical separation, but it is a conceptual distinction.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this type of division is based on the co-existence of different accidental qualities, not physical separation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes cutting from breaking?
A) Cutting is for soft bodies, breaking for hard bodies.
B) Cutting requires a tool, breaking does not.
C) Both A and B.
D) Cutting is faster.

Key Takeaway
Division in a body can occur physically (by breaking or cutting), by the difference of accidents (stable or unstable), or mentally (by estimation or supposition).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally divide the world into categories, do you ever stop to reflect that these mental divisions are real distinctions created by Allah? How does this awareness change the way you think about the unity of His creation?
?
The scholars distinguish between physical division and conceptual division. In your own life, when do you confuse a mental distinction with a physical separation? How might this mislead you in your understanding of yourself or your faith?
?
The author lists three types of division. Which of these do you most rely on in your daily thinking? Are you careful to recognize when a division is merely mental, and does that knowledge make you more humble before the oneness of Allah?

Obstacles to Physical Division
Lists obstacles to physical division and asserts mental division's limitlessness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various obstacles that can prevent the physical division of a body and why mental division is never hindered.

Core Idea
Physical division has limits; mental division has none.

In the previous discussion, division was classified into types. Now, the author addresses practical obstacles that can prevent the disintegrative kind of division from actually occurring. He also contrasts this with hypothetical mental division, which faces no such barriers. This lesson explores the factors that stop physical separation and introduces the unbounded nature of mental division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the examples given for obstacles.
The passage contrasts physical and mental division.
2
Identify the four obstacles
List the four factors that can prevent disintegrative division: substantial form, hardness, lack of tool, extreme smallness.
Remember that these obstacles only apply to physical division.
3
Reflect on the nature of mental division
The passage states that mental division never stops. Think about why this is true and how it differs from physical division.
Mental division is not limited by material constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم قد يمنع عن القسمة الانفكاكية مانع كصورة نوعية كما في الأفلاك، أو صلابة شديدة في بعض الأجسام العنصرية، أو فقد آلة يحتاج إليها في القطع، أو صغر متبالغ لا يعتبر معه القطع ولا الكسر. وأما القسمة الفرضية فلا تقف أبداً. وقد بين انحصار القسمة في الثلاثة المذكورة بأنها إما مؤدية إلى الافتراق وهي الفكية، أو لا. وحينئذ إما أن تكون موجبة للانفصال في الخارج وهي التي باختلاف عرضين، أو في الذهن وهي الوهمية. وإنما ذكر الفرض العقلي مع الوهم لأن الوهم ربما لم يقدر على تمييز طرف عن طرف لغاية الصغر فيقف، بخلاف العقل فإنه لا يقف لإحاطته بالكليات المشتملة على الكبير والصغير. والصواب: إن اختلاف الأعراض لا يوجب انفصالاً خارجياً، لأنا نعلم قطعاً أن الجسم المتصل في نفسه إذا وقع ضوء على بعضه لم ينفصل في الخارج حتى إذا زال الضوء عنه عاد إلى اتصاله، بل هذا الاختلاف باعث للوهم على فرض الأجزاء. وحينئذ يقال: الانفصال إما في الخارج كما بالقطع والكسر، وإما في الوهم.
Yes, a preventing factor may prevent disintegrative division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya), such as a substantial form as in the celestial spheres, or intense hardness in some elemental bodies, or lack of a tool needed for cutting, or extreme smallness such that cutting or breaking is not considered. As for mental division (al-qisma al-farḍiyya), it never stops. And it has been explained that the division is confined to the three mentioned types: either it leads to separation, which is the disintegrative type, or not. If it does not lead to separation, then it either necessitates external separation, which is that due to difference of accidents, or mental separation, which is the estimative type. He only mentioned mental supposition (al-fard al-ʿaqlī) along with estimation because estimation may not be able to distinguish one side from another due to extreme smallness, so it stops, unlike the intellect which does not stop because it encompasses universals that include the large and the small. The correct view is that difference of accidents does not cause external separation, for we know for certain that a body continuous in itself, when light falls on part of it, does not separate externally, and when the light disappears it returns to its continuity; rather, this difference prompts the estimation to suppose parts. At that point it is said: separation is either external, as by cutting and breaking, or mental.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous classification by introducing factors that can prevent physical (infikākiyya) division, such as substantial form, hardness, lack of tools, or extreme smallness. It then provides a more refined logical classification: division either leads to actual separation (the disintegrative type) or it does not. If it does not, it may still yield external separation by difference of accidents, or mental separation by estimation. The passage also clarifies why the author mentions intellectual supposition (al-fard al-ʿaqlī) alongside estimation: because estimation may fail to perceive very small differences, whereas the intellect can conceive of infinite division through universals. Finally, a crucial correction is made: difference of accidents does not produce real external separation. The example of light falling on part of a body is given: when light illuminates only part, the body does not split; it remains one. The difference in accidents only stimulates the mind to imagine parts. Therefore, true external separation occurs only by cutting or breaking, while mental separation occurs in estimation.

Key Terms
قسمة انفكاكية
قسمة فرضية
صورة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT mentioned as an obstacle to disintegrative division?
A) Substantial form
B) Intense hardness
C) Lack of a tool
D) Lack of intellectual capacity

Key Takeaway
Physical division can be prevented by four factors, but mental division is always possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as physical division can be stopped by obstacles like hardness or lack of a tool, what 'hardness' in your heart prevents you from breaking down your pride and submitting fully to Allah? Identify one obstacle from the passage and resolve today to remove it through sincere introspection.
?
The passage says mental division never stops. In your journey of faith, do you ever stop thinking deeply about Allah's names and attributes because you feel you've understood enough? How can you push yourself to never stop seeking knowledge, just as mental division has no end?

Refining Division and Correcting a Misconception
Refines division into three types and corrects a misconception about accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of division is refined into three types and why difference of accidents does not cause real physical separation.

Core Idea
Accidental differences only prompt the mind to divide; they do not actually split the body.

The author now provides a more precise classification of division, building on the earlier threefold division. He clarifies that one of those types—division by difference of accidents—does not result in actual physical separation, contrary to what might be assumed. This lesson unpacks the refined classification and corrects a common misunderstanding about how accidents relate to division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical flow: from initial classification to correction.
Pay attention to the distinction between external and mental separation.
2
Trace the classification tree
Map out the three types: infikākiyya (leads to separation), then the other two (not leading to separation): one by difference of accidents (external in appearance) and estimative (mental).
The correction alters the status of the 'difference of accidents' type.
3
Understand the role of estimation vs. intellect
The author explains why he mentions both estimation and intellectual supposition: estimation can fail with very small sizes, but intellect can always conceive of division.
This highlights the limits of estimation.
4
Grasp the correction about accidents
The correct view: difference of accidents does not cause real external separation. Use the light example to understand why.
Real external separation only occurs by cutting or breaking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم قد يمنع عن القسمة الانفكاكية مانع كصورة نوعية كما في الأفلاك، أو صلابة شديدة في بعض الأجسام العنصرية، أو فقد آلة يحتاج إليها في القطع، أو صغر متبالغ لا يعتبر معه القطع ولا الكسر. وأما القسمة الفرضية فلا تقف أبداً. وقد بين انحصار القسمة في الثلاثة المذكورة بأنها إما مؤدية إلى الافتراق وهي الفكية، أو لا. وحينئذ إما أن تكون موجبة للانفصال في الخارج وهي التي باختلاف عرضين، أو في الذهن وهي الوهمية. وإنما ذكر الفرض العقلي مع الوهم لأن الوهم ربما لم يقدر على تمييز طرف عن طرف لغاية الصغر فيقف، بخلاف العقل فإنه لا يقف لإحاطته بالكليات المشتملة على الكبير والصغير. والصواب: إن اختلاف الأعراض لا يوجب انفصالاً خارجياً، لأنا نعلم قطعاً أن الجسم المتصل في نفسه إذا وقع ضوء على بعضه لم ينفصل في الخارج حتى إذا زال الضوء عنه عاد إلى اتصاله، بل هذا الاختلاف باعث للوهم على فرض الأجزاء. وحينئذ يقال: الانفصال إما في الخارج كما بالقطع والكسر، وإما في الوهم.
Yes, a preventing factor may prevent disintegrative division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya), such as a substantial form as in the celestial spheres, or intense hardness in some elemental bodies, or lack of a tool needed for cutting, or extreme smallness such that cutting or breaking is not considered. The correct view is that difference of accidents does not cause external separation, for we know for certain that a body continuous in itself, when light falls on part of it, does not separate externally, and when the light disappears it returns to its continuity; rather, this difference prompts the estimation to suppose parts. At that point it is said: separation is either external, as by cutting and breaking, or mental.
